<!-- goidev:page=249 bbox=82.2,121.4,419.7,344.4 role=paragraph -->
7.5 The trauma of life: Freud The phenomenon that motivates Freud’s investigation in Beyond the Pleasure Principle is that of traumatic neurosis. The latter gives rise to a ‘compulsion to repeat’, wherein the sufferer compulsively relives the traumatic incident in his or her dreams. Yet if the function of dreams is primarily that of wish-fulfilment, in conformity with the pleasure prin-ciple, which strives to maximize pleasure – where pleasure is defined as a diminution of excitation – and to minimize displeasure – where dis-pleasure is defined as an increase in excitation – then traumatic neuro-sis poses a problem for psychoanalysis because it resists explanation in terms of the pleasure principle: why is the patient compelled to relive a shatteringly unpleasurable experience? Freud’s answer is that through this repetition, the psyche is striving to muster the anxiety required in order to achieve a successful binding ( Besetzung ) of the excess of excita-tion released by the traumatic breaching of its defences. It is this bind-ing that lies ‘beyond the pleasure principle’. The compulsion to repeat consists in an attempt on the part of the unconscious to relive the trau-matic incident in a condition of anxious anticipation that will allow it

<!-- goidev:page=249 bbox=82.2,109.4,397.2,118.4 role=footer -->
<!-- to buffer the shock, thereby compensating for the impotent terror that -->

<!-- goidev:page=249 bbox=82.2,614.1,94.2,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 234 -->

<!-- goidev:page=249 bbox=104.0,614.1,156.0,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Nihil Unbound -->

<!-- goidev:page=250 bbox=82.6,517.4,420.1,598.4 role=paragraph -->
disabled the organism and staunching the excessive influx of excita-tions brought about by a massive psychic wound. Moreover, insofar as the manifestations of the compulsion to repeat ‘give the appearance of some “daemonic” force at work’ (Freud 1991: 307), this is due to their inherently ‘instinctual’ ( Triebhaft ) character. In this regard, says Freud, the compulsion to repeat harbours the key to understanding the nature of the ‘drive’ ( Trieb ) as such:

<!-- goidev:page=250 bbox=94.6,325.4,427.6,502.4 role=paragraph -->
It seems then that a drive is an urge inherent in organic life to restore an earlier state of things which the living entity has been obliged to abandon under the pressure of external disturbing forces; that is, a kind of organic elasticity, or, to put it another way, the expression of the inertia inherent in organic life. […] It is possible to specify this final goal of all organic striving. It would be in contradiction to the conservative nature of the instincts if the goal of life were a state of things which had never been yet attained. On the contrary, it must be an old state of things, an initial state from which the living entity has at one time or another departed and to which it is striving to return by the circuitous paths along which its development leads. If we are to take it as a truth that knows no exception that everything living dies for internal reasons – becomes inorganic once again – then we shall be compelled to say that ‘ the aim of all life is death ’ and looking backwards, that ‘ inanimate things existed before living ones ’.

<!-- goidev:page=250 bbox=293.5,313.4,397.0,322.4 role=paragraph -->
(Freud 1991: 309–11)

<!-- goidev:page=250 bbox=82.6,121.5,447.1,298.4 role=paragraph -->
Thus, the fundamental tendency of the ‘drive’ or ‘instinct’ is the pri-mordial pull back towards the inorganic. Although life diverges from the inorganic in ever more circuitous detours, these are no more than temporary extensions of the latter, which will eventually contract back to their original inorganic condition, understood as the zero-degree of contraction, or decontraction . But if death constitutes the ‘aim of all life’, an aim which, according to Freud, is in some sense ‘internal’ to living organisms, this cannot simply be understood in Aristotelian terms as a telos , an intrinsic purpose orienting the development of the entity from within. A telos has no reality independently of the entities whose exis-tence it governs; thus, if the inorganic were merely the telos of the organic in this conventional sense, it could not possibly have existed before it. Yet Freud maintains the realist thesis according to which ‘inanimate things existed before living ones’ (my emphasis), and uses it to underwrite the reality of the death-drive. Consequently, the inor-

<!-- goidev:page=250 bbox=82.6,109.5,456.1,118.5 role=footer -->
<!-- ganic as ‘initial state’ and ‘aim’ of life cannot simply be understood as a -->

<!-- goidev:page=250 bbox=278.9,614.1,370.9,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- The Truth of Extinction -->

<!-- goidev:page=250 bbox=368.9,614.1,380.9,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 235 -->

<!-- goidev:page=251 bbox=82.2,121.4,428.7,598.4 role=paragraph -->
condition internal to the development of life, whether as the essence that life has been, or the telos which it will be. Just as the reality of the inorganic is not merely a function of the existence of the organic, so the reality of death is not merely a function of life’s past, or of its future. Death, understood as the principle of decontraction driving the con-tractions of organic life is not a past or future state towards which life tends, but rather the originary purposelessness which compels all pur-posefulness, whether organic or psychological. With the thesis that ‘the aim of all life is death’, Freud defuses Nietzsche’s metaphysics of the will: the life that wills power is merely a contraction of the death that wants nothing. The will to nothingness is not an avatar of the will to power; rather, the will to power is merely a mask of the will to nothingness. But this ‘nothingness’ cannot be retro-jected into the past or projected into the future; the only temporality commensurate with it is that of the ‘anterior posteriority’ proper to physical death as that which seizes organic temporality, but which can-not be seized by it. Thus, the repetition which is driven by death does not repeat the latter as though it were an earlier state of affairs experi-enced by life or consciousness, for the trauma which drives repetition is precisely what cannot be lived or consciously apprehended. Though trauma is real, its reality cannot be calibrated by the life of the organ-ism, just as it cannot be commensurated with the resources of con-sciousness. It can only be registered as a dysfunctioning of the organism, or as an interruption of consciousness, and it is this dysfunc-tion and this interruption that is repeated. Accordingly, it is because the ‘originary’ traumatic occurrence was only ever registered in the unconscious, rather than experienced, that there is a compulsion to (re-)experience it. But it can only be re-experienced as something that was neither lived nor experienced, since trauma marks the obliteration of life and experience. Nevertheless, the fact that experience cannot obliterate itself points to the reality of trauma, which cannot simply be construed as a function of experience. The reality of trauma is registered as an unconscious wound, which continues to resonate in the psychic economy as an unresolved distur-bance, an un-dampened excess of excitation. And it is because it indexes an influx of excitation vastly in excess of the binding capacities exer-cised by what Freud calls ‘the perception-consciousness system’ that trauma leaves behind this permanent imprint in the unconscious, since consciousness always arises instead of a memory trace. 42 Thus, it is not the traumatic experience (which never occurred), but rather this uncon-

<!-- goidev:page=251 bbox=82.2,109.4,392.7,118.4 role=footer -->
<!-- scious trace whose demand to be renegotiated gives rise to compulsive -->

<!-- goidev:page=251 bbox=82.2,614.1,94.2,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 236 -->

<!-- goidev:page=251 bbox=104.0,614.1,156.0,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Nihil Unbound -->

<!-- goidev:page=252 bbox=82.6,445.4,424.6,598.4 role=paragraph -->
repetition. Trauma is constitutively unconscious: it only exists as a trace. Yet this traumatic trace persists as a permanent and indelible imprint in the unconscious because it testifies to something unman-ageable for the filtering apparatus of the perception-consciousness sys-tem: a haemorrhaging of the psyche. Freud then proposes a remarkable speculative hypothesis linking the origins of this filtering apparatus to the genesis of organic individua-tion. A primitive organic vesicle (that is, a small bladder, cell, bubble or hollow structure) becomes capable of filtering the continuous and potentially lethal torrent of external stimuli by sacrificing part of itself in order to erect a protective shield against excessive influxes of excita-tion. In doing so, it effects a definitive separation between organic inte-riority and inorganic exteriority:

<!-- goidev:page=252 bbox=94.6,253.5,441.1,430.4 role=paragraph -->
[The vesicle] acquires the shield in this way: its outermost surface ceases to have the structure proper to living matter, becomes to some degree inorganic and thenceforth functions as a special envelope or membrane resistant to stimuli. In consequence, the energies of the external world are able to pass into the next underlying layers, which have remained living, with only a fragment of their original intensity […] By its death the outer layer has saved all the deeper ones from a similar fate – unless, that is to say, stimuli reach it which are so strong that they break through the protective shield. Protection against stim-uli is an almost more important function for the living organism than reception of stimuli […] In highly developed organisms the receptive cortical layers of the former vesicle has long been with-drawn into the depths of the interior of the body, though portions of it have been left behind on the surface immediately beneath the shield against stimuli.

<!-- goidev:page=252 bbox=308.4,241.5,384.9,250.5 role=paragraph -->
(Freud 1991: 299)

<!-- goidev:page=252 bbox=82.6,121.5,411.1,226.5 role=paragraph -->
Accordingly, the separation between organic interiority and inorganic exteriority is won at the cost of the death of part of the primitive organ ism itself, and it is this death that gives rise to the protective shield filtering out the potentially lethal influxes of external energy. Thus, individuated organic life is won at the cost of this aboriginal death whereby the organism first becomes capable of separating itself from the inorganic outside (cf. Chapter 2). This death, which gives birth to organic individuation, thereby conditions the possibility of organic phylogenesis, as well as of sexual reproduction. Consequently, not only

<!-- goidev:page=252 bbox=82.6,109.5,388.6,118.5 role=footer -->
<!-- does this death precede the organism, it is the precondition for the -->

<!-- goidev:page=252 bbox=278.9,614.1,370.9,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- The Truth of Extinction -->

<!-- goidev:page=252 bbox=368.9,614.1,380.9,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 237 -->

<!-- goidev:page=253 bbox=82.2,265.5,437.7,598.4 role=paragraph -->
organism’s ability to reproduce and die. If the death-drive qua compul-sion to repeat is the originary, primordial motive force driving organic life, this is because the motor of repetition – the repeating instance – is this trace of the aboriginal trauma of organic individuation. The death-drive understood as repetition of the inorganic is the repetition of the death which gave birth to the organism – a death that cannot be satis-factorily repeated, not only because the organism which bears its trace did not yet exist to experience it, but also because that trace is the marker of an exorbitant death, one that even in dying, the organism cannot successfully repeat. Thus, the trace of aboriginal death harbours an impossible demand for organic life: it is the trace of a trauma that demands to be integrated into the psychic economy of the organism, but which cannot because it expresses the originary traumatic scission between organic and inorganic. The organism cannot live the death that gives rise to the difference between life and death. The death-drive is the trace of this scission: a scission that will never be successfully bound (invested) because it remains the unbindable excess that makes binding possible. It is as the bearer of this scission and this excess that physical death cannot be located either at the origin or end of life. Decontraction is not a negentropic starting point to which one could return, or an entropic terminus towards which one could hasten. Its reality is that of the ‘being-nothing’ whose anterior posteriority expresses the identity of entropic indifference and negentropic differ-ence, an identity which is given to thought as the objective reality that already determines it. This determination occurs through philosophy’s binding of the trauma of extinction, which persists as an un-conscious and un-bound disturbance of phenomenal consciousness, fuelling the will to know.

<!-- goidev:page=253 bbox=82.2,121.4,428.7,248.4 role=paragraph -->
7.6 Binding extinction Extinction is real yet not empirical, since it is not of the order of expe-rience. It is transcendental yet not ideal, since it coincides with the external objectification of thought unfolding at a specific historical juncture when the resources of intelligibility, and hence the lexicon of ideality, are being renegotiated. In this regard, it is precisely the extinc-tion of meaning that clears the way for the intelligibility of extinction. Senselessness and purposelessness are not merely privative; they repre-sent a gain in intelligibility. The cancellation of sense, purpose, and possibility marks the point at which the ‘horror’ concomitant with the

<!-- goidev:page=253 bbox=82.2,109.4,410.7,118.4 role=footer -->
<!-- impossibility of either being or not-being becomes intelligible. Thus, if -->

<!-- goidev:page=253 bbox=82.2,614.1,94.2,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 238 -->

<!-- goidev:page=253 bbox=104.0,614.1,156.0,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Nihil Unbound -->

<!-- goidev:page=254 bbox=82.6,433.4,411.1,598.4 role=paragraph -->
everything is dead already, this is not only because extinction disables those possibilities which were taken to be constitutive of life and existence, but also because the will to know is driven by the traumatic reality of extinction, and strives to become equal to the trauma of the in-itself whose trace it bears. In becoming equal to it, philosophy achieves a binding of extinction, through which the will to know is finally rendered commensurate with the in-itself. This binding coin-cides with the objectification of thinking understood as the adequation without correspondence between the objective reality of extinction and the subjective knowledge of the trauma to which it gives rise. It is this adequation that constitutes the truth of extinction. But to acknowledge this truth, the subject of philosophy must also recognize that he or she is already dead, and that philosophy is neither a medium of affirmation nor a source of justification, but rather the organon of extinction.

<!-- goidev:page=254 bbox=278.9,614.1,370.9,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- The Truth of Extinction -->

<!-- goidev:page=254 bbox=368.9,614.1,380.9,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 239 -->

<!-- goidev:page=255 bbox=225.3,92.8,237.3,100.8 role=pagenumber -->

- 10. In this regard, the ‘state space semantics’ which Churchland has elaborated

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,520.1,398.6,528.1 role=paragraph -->
must make do with a criterion of similarity. But he cannot provide a crite-

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,510.1,398.6,518.1 role=paragraph -->
rion of similarity of meaning for his psychological prototypes without sur-

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,500.1,402.6,508.1 role=paragraph -->
reptiously resorting to an empiricist account of the identity conditions for

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,490.1,394.6,498.1 role=paragraph -->
concepts. As a result, the semantic dimensions which are supposed to indi-

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,480.1,410.6,488.1 role=paragraph -->
viduate concepts are simply stipulated with reference to empirical features of

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,470.1,366.6,478.1 role=paragraph -->
the environment, and concepts become compositionally defined on the

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,460.1,386.6,468.1 role=paragraph -->
basis of empirical simples. According to Fodor and Lepore, this presents

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,450.1,386.6,458.1 role=paragraph -->
Churchland with the following dilemma: he can either re-invoke some ver-

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,440.1,418.6,448.1 role=paragraph -->
sion of the analytic–synthetic distinction or embrace Humean ‘blank slate’

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,430.1,390.6,438.1 role=paragraph -->
empiricism. Churchland has retorted that his account can indeed provide a

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,420.1,386.6,428.1 role=paragraph -->
robust notion of functional similarity for prototypes via discriminatory

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,410.1,386.6,418.1 role=paragraph -->
mechanisms while avoiding the twin perils of associationism and the ana-

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,400.1,422.6,408.1 role=paragraph -->
lytic a priori. Cf. J. Fodor and E. Lepore (1996a), ‘Churchland and State Space

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,390.1,414.6,398.1 role=paragraph -->
Semantics’ in McCauley ed. 1996, 145–58; P. M. Churchland (1996a), ‘Fodor

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,380.1,382.6,388.1 role=paragraph -->
and Lepore: State Space Semantics and Meaning Holism’ in McCauley ed.

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,370.1,398.6,378.1 role=paragraph -->
1996, 273–7; J. Fodor and E. Lepore (1996b), ‘Reply to Churchland’ in

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,360.1,390.6,368.1 role=paragraph -->
McCauley ed. 1996, 159–62; P. M. Churchland (1996b), ‘Second Reply to

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,350.1,386.6,358.1 role=paragraph -->
Fodor and Lepore’ in McCauley ed. 1996, 278–83; and P. M. Churchland

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,340.1,377.7,348.1 role=paragraph -->
(1998a) ‘Conceptual Similarity across Sensory and Neural Diversity: The

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,330.1,386.3,338.1 role=paragraph -->
Fodor–Lepore Challenge Answered’ in P. M. Churchland and P. S. Churchland

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,320.1,406.6,328.1 role=paragraph -->
1998, 81–112. For a defence of Churchland in this controversy, see Jesse J.

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,310.1,347.5,318.1 role=paragraph -->
Prinz, ‘Empiricism and State Space Semantics’ in Paul Churchland , Brian L.

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,300.1,362.6,308.1 role=paragraph -->
Keeley ed. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2006, 88–112.

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=82.6,290.1,210.6,298.1 role=citation -->
- 11. A version of this so-called

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=82.6,290.1,376.3,298.1 role=paragraph -->
reductio of EM is expounded at length by Lynn

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,280.1,394.4,288.1 role=paragraph -->
Rudder-Baker in Saving Belief: A Critique of Physicalism , Princeton, NJ: Princeton

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,270.1,426.6,278.1 role=paragraph -->
University Press, 1987. Michael Devitt – an ardent opponent of eliminativism –

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,260.1,398.6,268.1 role=paragraph -->
provides a convincing refutation of this line of attack in his 1990 article

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,250.1,359.5,258.1 role=paragraph -->
‘Transcendentalism about Content’, Pacific Philosophical Quaterly , Vol. 71,

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,240.1,390.6,248.1 role=paragraph -->
No. 2, 247–63. Despite this and several equally decisive refutations by

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,230.1,390.6,238.1 role=paragraph -->
both Paul Churchland (cf. 1981, 1993) and Patricia Churchland (1981), the

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,220.1,426.6,228.1 role=paragraph -->
‘self-defeating’ objection is regularly trotted out by foes of eliminativism.

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=82.6,210.1,414.6,218.1 role=citation -->
- 12. The example is from Patricia Churchland, ‘Is Determinism Self-Refuting?’ in

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,200.1,342.6,208.1 role=paragraph -->
Mind , 90, 1981, 99–101; cited in P. M. Churchland (1989), 22.

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=82.6,190.1,398.6,198.1 role=citation -->
- 13. In his ‘Reply to Glymour’, Churchland expands on this claim: ‘Ceteris

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,180.1,418.6,188.1 role=paragraph -->
paribus, an activated prototype [i.e. an explanation] is better if it is part of

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,170.1,390.6,178.1 role=paragraph -->
the most unified conceptual configuration […] networks that have formed

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,160.1,410.6,168.1 role=paragraph -->
the simplest or most unified partitions across their activation space are net-

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,150.1,390.6,158.1 role=paragraph -->
works that do much better at generalising their knowledge to novel cases.

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,140.1,410.6,148.1 role=paragraph -->
Very briefly, they do better at recognising novel situations for what they are

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,130.1,390.6,138.1 role=paragraph -->
because they have generated a relevantly unified similarity gradient that

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,120.1,410.6,128.1 role=paragraph -->
will catch novel cases in the same subvolume that catches the training case’

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=98.6,110.1,222.6,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- (P. M. Churchland, 1998c: 286). -->

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=341.5,614.1,361.5,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=256 bbox=368.9,614.1,380.9,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 241 -->

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=82.2,590.1,422.2,598.1 role=paragraph -->
14. ‘FP, like any other theory, is a family of learned vectorial prototypes, proto-

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,580.1,414.2,588.1 role=paragraph -->
types that sustain recognition of current reality, anticipation of future real-

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=82.2,560.1,410.2,578.1 role=paragraph -->
ity, and manipulation of ongoing reality’ (P. M. Churchland, 1998b: 15). 15. ‘Whether FP is false and whether it will fail to reduce are empirical issues

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,550.1,386.2,558.1 role=paragraph -->
whose decisive settlement must flow from experimental research and theo-

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,540.1,394.2,548.1 role=paragraph -->
retical development, not from any arguments a priori’ (P. M. Churchland,

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=82.2,520.1,422.2,538.1 role=paragraph -->
1998b: 10). 16. ‘[T]he folk-semantical notion of “reference” is without any real integrity.

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,510.1,410.2,518.1 role=paragraph -->
Reference is uniquely fixed neither by networks of belief, nor by causal rela-

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,500.1,406.2,508.1 role=paragraph -->
tions, nor by anything else, because there is no single uniform relation that

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,490.1,406.2,498.1 role=paragraph -->
connects each descriptive term to the world in anything like the fashion that

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,480.1,334.2,488.1 role=paragraph -->
common sense supposes’ (P. M. Churchland, 1989: 276–7).

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=82.2,470.1,414.2,478.1 role=citation -->
- 17. Thus, Churchland invokes Einstein’s Special Theory of Relativity to underline

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,460.1,406.2,468.1 role=paragraph -->
the extent to which ‘new theories often bring with them a novel and propri-

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,450.1,398.2,458.1 role=paragraph -->
etary vocabulary for describing the observable world, a vocabulary that can

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,440.1,410.2,448.1 role=paragraph -->
augment or even displace the old observational vocabulary’ (P. M. Churchland

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,430.1,142.2,438.1 role=paragraph -->
1998b: 18).

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=82.2,420.1,390.2,428.1 role=citation -->
- 18. Churchland himself frequently adduces the argument that Ptolemaic astron-

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,410.1,394.2,418.1 role=paragraph -->
omy could have happily continued ‘explaining’ and accommodating recal-

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,400.1,414.2,408.1 role=paragraph -->
citrant astronomical data by piling virtual epicycle upon virtual epicycle. Cf.

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,390.1,406.2,398.1 role=paragraph -->
for instance P. M. Churchland’s ‘Densmore and Dennett on Virtual Machines

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,380.1,258.2,388.1 role=paragraph -->
and Consciousness’ in Philosophy and Phenomenological Research , Vol. LIX,

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=98.2,370.1,166.2,378.1 role=paragraph -->
No. 3, 1999, 767.

<!-- goidev:page=257 bbox=82.2,360.1,166.2,368.1 role=citation -->

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=82.6,479.1,257.3,489.1 role=paragraph -->
2 The Thanatosis of Enlightenment

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=87.2,460.1,243.2,468.1 role=citation -->
- 1. Theodore Adorno and Max Horkheimer,

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=269.3,460.1,386.8,468.1 role=paragraph -->
Dialectic of Enlightenment tr.

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,450.1,350.6,458.1 role=paragraph -->
Edmund Jephcott, Stanford, CA: Stanford University Press, 2002.

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=87.2,440.1,431.2,448.1 role=citation -->
- 2. Andreas Huyssen distinguishes these five registers as follows: ‘[F]irst, in rela-

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,430.1,402.6,438.1 role=paragraph -->
tion to the critique of the commodity form and its powers of reification and

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,420.1,387.7,428.1 role=paragraph -->
deception, a thoroughly negative form of mimesis [ Mimesis ans Verhärtete ];

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,410.1,378.6,418.1 role=paragraph -->
secondly, in relation to the anthropological grounding of human nature

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,400.1,246.6,408.1 role=paragraph -->
which, as Adorno insists in Minima Moralia , is ‘indissolubly linked to imita-

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,390.1,414.6,398.1 role=paragraph -->
tion’; third, in a biological somatic sense geared toward survival, as Adorno

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,380.1,402.6,388.1 role=paragraph -->
had encountered it in Roger Caillois’s work […]; fourth, in the Freudian

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,370.1,356.5,378.1 role=paragraph -->
sense of identification and projection indebted to Totem and Taboo ; and,

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,360.1,418.6,368.1 role=paragraph -->
lastly, in an aesthetic sense that resonates strongly with Benjamin’s language

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,350.1,410.6,358.1 role=paragraph -->
theory, as it relates to the role of word and image in the evolution of signi-

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,340.1,374.2,348.1 role=paragraph -->
fying systems.’ Andreas Huyssen, ‘Of Mice and Mimesis’, New German

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,330.1,355.1,338.1 role=paragraph -->
Critique , No. 81, Dialectic of Enlightenment (Autumn 2000), 66–7.

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=87.2,320.1,439.2,328.1 role=citation -->
- 3. Adorno had reviewed Caillois’s 1934 text ‘La Mante religieuse’ (originally pub-

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,310.1,300.6,318.1 role=paragraph -->
lished in Minotaure 5 [1934]: 23–6) in the Zeitschrift für Sozialforschung 7 (1938):

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,300.1,442.6,308.1 role=paragraph -->
410–11. Also relevant in this regard is Caillois’s ‘Mimétisme et psychasthénie

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,290.1,406.3,298.1 role=paragraph -->
légendaire’, originally published in Minotaure 7 (1935): 4–10, which we will dis-

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,280.1,373.1,288.1 role=paragraph -->
cuss below. Both texts are included in Caillois’s L’Homme et le sacré , Paris:

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,270.1,422.6,278.1 role=paragraph -->
Gallimard, Folio/Éssais, 1988 (first published in 1938). English versions can be

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,260.1,388.9,268.1 role=paragraph -->
found in On the Edge of Surrealism: A Roger Caillois Reader , C. Frank and C. Nash

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,250.1,274.6,258.1 role=paragraph -->
(eds), Durham: Duke University Press, 2003.

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=87.2,240.1,171.2,248.1 role=citation -->
- 4. We will return to

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=168.1,240.1,383.0,248.1 role=paragraph -->
Beyond the Pleasure Principle in Chapter 7, where we will

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,230.1,290.6,238.1 role=paragraph -->
reconsider Freud’s account of the death-drive.

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=87.2,220.1,331.2,228.1 role=citation -->
- 5. Cf. Sigmund Freud, ‘Beyond the Pleasure Principle’ in

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=314.5,220.1,382.5,228.1 role=paragraph -->
The Penguin Freud

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,210.1,242.6,218.1 role=paragraph -->
Library. Vol. 11: On Metapsychology , Harmondsworth, Middlesex: Penguin,

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,200.1,162.6,208.1 role=paragraph -->
1991, 270–338.

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=87.2,190.1,391.2,198.1 role=citation -->
- 6. The implication, more pathetic than provocative, is unavoidable: Einstein

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.6,180.1,318.6,188.1 role=paragraph -->
and Himmler are separated merely by degrees, not kind.

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=87.2,170.1,159.2,178.1 role=citation -->
- 7. Jay Bernstein,

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=87.2,170.1,386.8,178.1 role=paragraph -->

- 8. Roger Caillois,

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=87.2,150.1,407.2,158.1 role=citation -->

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.7,140.1,238.7,148.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=82.7,130.1,358.7,138.1 role=citation -->

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=345.0,130.1,385.0,138.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=258 bbox=98.7,120.1,286.7,128.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,590.1,390.8,598.1 role=paragraph -->
tr. N. Metzel, Evanston: Northwestern University Press, 1970; La Schizophrénie ,

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=82.2,570.1,400.5,588.1 role=paragraph -->
Paris: Payot, Rivages, 1997 (originally published 1927).  11. Phagocytosis is a process describing the engulfment and destruction of extra-

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,560.1,390.2,568.1 role=paragraph -->
cellularly derived materials by phagocytic cells, such as macrophages and

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,550.1,146.2,558.1 role=paragraph -->
neutrophils.

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=82.2,540.1,362.2,548.1 role=citation -->
- 12. Roger Caillois, ‘Mimétisme et psychasthénie légendaire’ in

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=329.4,540.1,389.4,548.1 role=paragraph -->
L’Homme et le

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,530.1,230.2,538.1 role=paragraph -->
sacré , Gallimard, Folio/Éssais, 1988.

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=82.2,520.1,402.2,528.1 role=citation -->
- 13. In this regard, the veritable analogue for the dialectic of enlightenment is

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,510.1,350.9,518.1 role=paragraph -->
not Homer’s Odyssey but rather David Cronenberg’s The Fly (1986), whose

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,500.1,414.2,508.1 role=paragraph -->
protagonist declares: ‘I was an insect who dreamed he was a man – and loved

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,490.1,346.2,498.1 role=paragraph -->
it – but now the dream is over and the insect is awake.’

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=82.2,462.1,197.2,472.1 role=paragraph -->
3 The Enigma of Realism

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=86.7,443.1,162.7,451.1 role=citation -->
- 1. Q. Meillassoux,

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=86.7,443.1,389.3,451.1 role=paragraph -->
Après la finitude. Éssai sur la nécéssité de la contingence , Paris:

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=86.7,423.1,442.7,441.1 role=paragraph -->
Seuil, 2006, 39. 2. ‘Billion’ and ‘trillion’ will be used throughout, following their now interna-

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,413.1,386.2,421.1 role=paragraph -->
tionally accepted US usage, as meaning a thousand million and a million,

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,403.1,182.2,411.1 role=paragraph -->
million respectively.

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=86.7,393.1,390.7,401.1 role=citation -->
- 3. Graham Harman has elaborated a profound critique of this tendency in con-

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,383.1,398.2,391.1 role=paragraph -->
temporary philosophy, seeing in it an avatar of a generalized anti-realism.

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,373.1,386.2,381.1 role=paragraph -->
Whether the relation in question is the epistemological relation between

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,363.1,382.2,371.1 role=paragraph -->
mind and world, the phenomenological relation between noesis and noema,

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,353.1,358.3,361.1 role=paragraph -->
the ekstatic relation between Sein and Dasein , the prehensive relation

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,343.1,390.2,351.1 role=paragraph -->
between event-objects, or the processual relation between matter and mem-

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,333.1,394.2,341.1 role=paragraph -->
ory, Harman argues that this premium on relationality occludes the discon-

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,323.1,426.2,331.1 role=paragraph -->
tinuous reality of objects in favour of their reciprocal idealizations. Harman’s

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,313.1,406.2,321.1 role=paragraph -->
startlingly original interpretation of Heidegger provides the point of depar-

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,303.1,386.2,311.1 role=paragraph -->
ture for his complete re-orientation of phenomenology away from the pri-

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,293.1,362.2,301.1 role=paragraph -->
macy of the human relation to things and towards things themselves

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,283.1,366.2,291.1 role=paragraph -->
considered independently of their relation to humans or each other.

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,273.1,418.2,281.1 role=paragraph -->
Accordingly, the fundamental task for this ‘object-oriented philosophy’ con-

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,263.1,378.2,271.1 role=paragraph -->
sists in explaining how autonomous objects can ever interact with each

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,253.1,390.2,261.1 role=paragraph -->
other, and to that end Harman has developed a particularly ingenious the-

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,243.1,438.2,251.1 role=paragraph -->
ory of ‘vicarious causation’. Harman first outlines the rudiments of this project

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,233.1,382.5,241.1 role=paragraph -->
in Tool-Being: Heidegger and the Metaphysics of Objects , Chicago: Open Court,

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,223.1,274.2,231.1 role=paragraph -->
2002 and develops it further in Guerrilla Metaphysics: Phenomenology and the

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,213.1,214.2,221.1 role=paragraph -->
Carpentry of Things , Chicago: Open Court, 2005.

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=86.7,203.1,406.7,211.1 role=citation -->
- 4. Here are some paradigmatic expressions of the correlationist credo from three

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=98.2,193.1,378.2,201.1 role=paragraph -->
canonical continental philosophers: Nietzsche, Husserl, and Heidegger:

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=106.2,169.1,414.2,177.1 role=paragraph -->
Against the scientific prejudice – The biggest fable of all is the fable of

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=106.2,159.1,378.2,167.1 role=paragraph -->
knowledge. One would like to know what things-in-themselves are; but

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=106.2,149.1,374.2,157.1 role=paragraph -->
behold, there are no things-in-themselves! But even supposing there

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=106.2,139.1,395.8,147.1 role=paragraph -->
were an in-itself, an unconditioned thing, it would for that very reason

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=106.2,129.1,381.2,137.1 role=paragraph -->
be unknowable! […] [S]omething that is of no concern to anyone is not

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=106.2,119.1,134.2,127.1 role=paragraph -->
at all.

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=297.1,109.1,389.1,117.1 role=footer -->
<!-- (Nietzsche 1968: §555) -->

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=82.2,614.1,94.2,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 244 -->

<!-- goidev:page=259 bbox=104.0,614.1,124.0,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,590.1,393.0,598.1 role=paragraph -->
The existence of Nature cannot be the condition for the existence of con-

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,580.1,414.6,588.1 role=paragraph -->
sciousness, since Nature itself turns out to be a correlate of consciousness:

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,570.1,382.3,578.1 role=paragraph -->
Nature is only as being constituted in regular concatenations of con-

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,560.1,150.6,568.1 role=paragraph -->
sciousness.

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=310.2,550.1,386.2,558.1 role=paragraph -->
(Husserl 1982: 116)

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,535.1,390.6,543.1 role=paragraph -->
Given in and through this liberation [from the natural attitude] is the

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,525.1,398.6,533.1 role=paragraph -->
discovery of the universal, absolutely self-enclosed and absolutely self-

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,515.1,414.6,523.1 role=paragraph -->
sufficient correlation between the world itself and world-consciousness [...]

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,505.1,394.6,513.1 role=paragraph -->
the absolute correlation between beings of every sort and every meaning,

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,495.1,390.6,503.1 role=paragraph -->
on the one hand, and absolute subjectivity, as constituting meaning and

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,485.1,414.6,493.1 role=paragraph -->
ontic validity in this broadest manner, on the other hand. [...] [D]uring the

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,473.1,390.5,483.1 role=paragraph -->
consistently carried-out epoche  , [the world] is under our gaze purely as

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,465.1,390.6,473.1 role=paragraph -->
the correlate of the subjectivity which gives it ontic meaning, through

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,455.1,278.6,463.1 role=paragraph -->
whose validities the world ‘is’ at all.

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=301.6,445.1,393.6,453.1 role=paragraph -->
(Husserl 1970: 151–2)

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,430.1,397.8,438.1 role=paragraph -->
Of course only as long as Dasein is (that is, as long as an understanding of

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,420.1,422.6,428.1 role=paragraph -->
Being is ontically possible), ‘is there’ Being. When Dasein does not exist,

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,410.1,498.6,418.1 role=paragraph -->
‘independence’ ‘is’ not either, nor ‘is’ the ‘in-itself’. In such a case this sort

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,400.1,402.6,408.1 role=paragraph -->
of thing can be neither understood nor not understood. In such a case even

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,390.1,385.4,398.1 role=paragraph -->
entities within-the-world can neither be discovered nor lie hidden. In such

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=106.6,380.1,425.3,388.1 role=paragraph -->
a case it cannot be said that entities are, nor can it be said that they are not.

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=300.3,370.1,384.3,378.1 role=paragraph -->
(Heidegger 1962: 255)

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=87.2,349.1,163.2,357.1 role=citation -->
- 5. Q. Meillassoux,

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=87.2,349.1,311.2,357.1 role=paragraph -->
After Finitude: An Essay on the Necessity of Contingency , tr.

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,339.1,246.7,347.1 role=paragraph -->
R. Brassier, London: Continuum 2008.

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=88.2,329.1,384.2,337.1 role=citation -->
- 6. At the time of writing (2007), these objections have only been communi-

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,319.1,414.7,327.1 role=paragraph -->
cated verbally or in writing to Meillassoux; they have yet to appear in print.

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=87.2,309.1,211.2,317.1 role=citation -->
- 7. This is essentially Slavoj Z

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=200.5,309.1,406.1,319.6 role=paragraph -->
B iz B ek’s position: ‘[T]he only way effectively to

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,299.1,398.7,307.1 role=paragraph -->
account for the status of (self-)consciousness is to assert the ontological

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,289.1,458.7,297.1 role=paragraph -->
incompleteness of “reality” itself; there is “reality” only insofar as there is an

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,279.1,434.7,287.1 role=paragraph -->
ontological “gap”, a “crack”, in its very heart, that is to say, a traumatic

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,269.1,386.0,279.6 role=paragraph -->
excess, a foreign body which cannot be integrated into it’ (Z B iz B ek 2006: 242).

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,259.1,406.7,267.1 role=paragraph -->
The thesis that consciousness or subjectivity is not a substantial entity but

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,249.1,418.7,257.1 role=paragraph -->
rather an insubstantial gap fissuring the ontological order lies at the heart of

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,239.1,397.8,249.6 role=paragraph -->
Z B iz B ek’s (brilliant) hybridization of Lacan and Hegel. To his considerable

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,229.1,422.7,237.1 role=paragraph -->
credit, and in conformity with his commitment to a ‘dialectical materialism’,

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,219.1,380.5,229.6 role=paragraph -->
Z B iz B ek has consistently engaged with cognitive science (cf. Z B iz B ek 2006:

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,209.1,419.5,219.6 role=paragraph -->
146–250). However, it is difficult to square Z B iz B ek’s putative ‘materialism’ with

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,199.1,410.7,207.1 role=paragraph -->
his assertion that reality itself is structured around the traumatic kernel of

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,189.1,438.7,197.1 role=paragraph -->
subjectivity. If reality in-itself is necessarily constituted in relation to the fis-

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,179.1,410.7,187.1 role=paragraph -->
sure of self-consciousness, then all those material processes which, according

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,169.1,394.7,177.1 role=paragraph -->
to Darwin, preceded the emergence of self-consciousness, must be dismissed

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,159.1,402.7,167.1 role=paragraph -->
as phantasmatic ‘false memories’ generated by a delirious transcendental

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,149.1,134.7,157.1 role=paragraph -->
subject.

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=87.2,139.1,375.2,147.1 role=citation -->
- 8. I am indebted to Graham Harman, Robin Mackay, and Damian Veal for all

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,129.1,186.7,137.1 role=paragraph -->
these critical points.

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=87.2,119.1,387.2,127.1 role=citation -->
- 9. Though Kant would certainly not endorse an instrumentalist conception of

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=98.7,109.1,378.7,117.1 role=footer -->
<!-- science, it is his formula which most succinctly summarizes the way in -->

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=341.5,614.1,361.5,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=260 bbox=368.9,614.1,380.9,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 245 -->

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,590.1,386.2,598.1 role=paragraph -->
which correlationists render the empirical reality of scientific objects

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,580.1,402.2,588.1 role=paragraph -->
dependent upon transcendental conditions of objectivation: ‘The conditions

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,570.1,414.2,578.1 role=paragraph -->
of the possibility of experience in general are likewise conditions of the pos-

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,560.1,346.2,568.1 role=paragraph -->
sibility of the objects of experience’ (Kant: A 158/B 197).

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=82.2,540.1,398.2,558.1 role=citation -->
- 10. I owe this expression to Graham Harman. 11. We shall attempt to elaborate some of the conceptual resources required for

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,530.1,394.2,538.1 role=paragraph -->
such a task via a critical discussion of the work of François Laruelle in

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=82.2,510.1,410.2,528.1 role=paragraph -->
Chapter 5. 12. ‘In general, our consideration of the nature of contradiction has shown that

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,500.1,410.2,508.1 role=paragraph -->
it is not, so to speak, a blemish, an imperfection or a defect in something if

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,490.1,390.2,498.1 role=paragraph -->
a contradiction can be pointed out in it. On the contrary, every concrete

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,480.1,406.2,488.1 role=paragraph -->
thing, every Notion, is essentially a unity of distinguished and distinguish-

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,470.1,390.2,478.1 role=paragraph -->
able moments, which by virtue of their determinate, essential difference,

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,460.1,382.2,468.1 role=paragraph -->
pass over into contradictory moments. This contradictory side of course

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,450.1,398.2,458.1 role=paragraph -->
resolves itself into nothing, it withdraws into its negative unity. Now the

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,440.1,418.2,448.1 role=paragraph -->
thing, the subject, the Notion, is just this negative unity itself; it is inher-

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,430.1,410.2,438.1 role=paragraph -->
ently self-contradictory, but is no less the contradiction resolved: it is the

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,420.1,394.2,428.1 role=paragraph -->
ground that contains and supports its determinations’ (Hegel 1989: 442).

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=82.2,410.1,350.2,418.1 role=citation -->
- 13. Indeed, though Heidegger and Wittgenstein are probably the most

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,400.1,390.2,408.1 role=paragraph -->
renowned exemplars of this resurgence of religiosity in twentieth-century

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,390.1,390.2,398.1 role=paragraph -->
European philosophy, they are far from unusual in this regard. Twentieth-

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,380.1,390.2,388.1 role=paragraph -->
Century European philosophy harbours what can only be described as a pro-

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,370.1,386.2,378.1 role=paragraph -->
foundly conflicted attitude towards Judeo-Christian monotheism. Thus the

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,360.1,382.2,368.1 role=paragraph -->
avowed atheism of figures such as Bachelard, Badiou, Carnap, Cavailles,

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,350.1,390.2,358.1 role=paragraph -->
Neurath, Reichenbach, Sartre, Schlick, Deleuze, and Waismann is more than

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,340.1,370.2,348.1 role=paragraph -->
counterbalanced by the theological overtones in the works of Adorno,

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,330.1,382.2,338.1 role=paragraph -->
Benjamin, Bloch, Derrida, Gadamer, Henry, Horkheimer, Jaspers, Levinas,

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,320.1,386.2,328.1 role=paragraph -->
Marion, Merleau-Ponty, Ricoeur, and Scheler. The latter all seem to hold

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,310.1,394.2,318.1 role=paragraph -->
Judaeo-Christian theology in far higher philosophical regard than the cog-

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,300.1,394.2,308.1 role=paragraph -->
nitive achievements of modern scientific rationality. Husserl and the neo-

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,290.1,374.2,298.1 role=paragraph -->
Kantians hold an equivocal position here: like Kant before them, they

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,280.1,426.2,288.1 role=paragraph -->
declared their allegiance to the ideals of scientific rationality; yet at the same

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,270.1,390.2,278.1 role=paragraph -->
time their understanding of the latter seems to have been wholly compati-

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,260.1,378.2,268.1 role=paragraph -->
ble with, or at least did not rule out, an embrace of Judaeo-Christian

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,250.1,410.2,258.1 role=paragraph -->
monotheism – hence Cohen’s Judaism, Husserl’s Lutheranism, and so on. In

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,240.1,410.2,248.1 role=paragraph -->
this regard, their stance typifies post-Kantian fideism: since neither God’s

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,230.1,394.2,238.1 role=paragraph -->
existence nor his non-existence can be rationally demonstrated, it is per-

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,220.1,394.2,228.1 role=paragraph -->
fectly possible to reconcile a commitment to scientific rationality with a

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,210.1,394.2,218.1 role=paragraph -->
commitment to Judaism or Christianity. It is worth mentioning in this con-

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,200.1,418.2,208.1 role=paragraph -->
nection the striking similarities between Meillassoux’s attack on correlation-

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,190.1,405.5,198.1 role=paragraph -->
ist fideism in Après la finitude and Lenin’s assault on clericalist idealism in

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,180.1,397.4,188.1 role=paragraph -->
Materialism and Empirio-criticism (originally published 1908, tr. A. Fineberg,

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,170.1,398.2,178.1 role=paragraph -->
Peking: Foreign Languages Press, 1972); especially in Chapter 1, sections 2

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,160.1,418.2,168.1 role=paragraph -->
and 3, where Lenin lambasts the ‘correlativist’ theory of subject and object

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,150.1,398.2,158.1 role=paragraph -->
which he explicitly connects to ‘fideism’. My thanks to Damian Veal for

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,140.1,366.2,148.1 role=paragraph -->
pointing these parallels out to me. Though he does not mention him,

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,130.1,426.2,138.1 role=paragraph -->
Lenin’s tract may well have provided a source of inspiration for Meillassoux’s

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,120.1,410.2,128.1 role=paragraph -->
book. That the ‘correlativism’ excoriated by Lenin in 1908 remains in full

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=98.2,110.1,398.2,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- force a hundred years later is both a testament to the continuing relevance -->

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=82.2,614.1,94.2,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 246 -->

<!-- goidev:page=261 bbox=104.0,614.1,124.0,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,590.1,394.6,598.1 role=paragraph -->
of Lenin’s intervention and a depressing reminder of mainstream academic

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,580.1,400.5,588.1 role=paragraph -->
philosophy’s seemingly imperturbable idealism. Whether or not Après la finitude

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,570.1,230.6,578.1 role=paragraph -->
was partly inspired by Materialism and Empirio-Criticism , Meillassoux’s pro-

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,560.1,418.6,568.1 role=paragraph -->
foundly original speculative alternative to correlationism quells any suggestion

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,550.1,154.6,558.1 role=paragraph -->
of imitation.

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=82.6,540.1,414.6,548.1 role=citation -->
- 14. In Meillassoux’s eyes, the fact that the third thesis is not derived directly

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,530.1,390.6,538.1 role=paragraph -->
from the principle of factuality but relies on independent considerations

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,520.1,382.6,528.1 role=paragraph -->
renders it considerably more precarious than the first two. Meillassoux

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,510.1,390.6,518.1 role=paragraph -->
makes it clear that he views this as a shortcoming which he hopes to rem-

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=82.6,490.1,418.6,508.1 role=paragraph -->
edy in future work. Cf. Meillasoux, Après la finitude , 152  3.  15. ‘Freedom is not simply the opposite of deterministic causal necessity: as Kant

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,480.1,410.6,488.1 role=paragraph -->
knew, it means a specific mode of causality; the agent’s self-determination.

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,470.1,394.6,478.1 role=paragraph -->
There is in fact a kind of Kantian antinomy of freedom: if an act is fully

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,460.1,398.6,468.1 role=paragraph -->
determined by preceding causes, it is, of course, not free; if, however, it

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,450.1,386.6,458.1 role=paragraph -->
depends on the pure contingency which momentarily severs the full causal

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,440.1,410.6,448.1 role=paragraph -->
chain, it is also not free. The only way to resolve this antinomy is to intro-

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,430.1,390.6,438.1 role=paragraph -->
duce a second-order reflexive causality: I am determined by causes (be it

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,420.1,402.6,428.1 role=paragraph -->
direct brute natural causes or motivations), and the space of freedom is not

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,410.1,402.6,418.1 role=paragraph -->
a magic gap in this first-level causal chain but my ability retroactively to

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,400.1,383.0,410.6 role=paragraph -->
choose/determine which causes will determine me’ (Z B iz B ek 2006: 203). In

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.6,390.1,389.8,400.6 role=paragraph -->
Z B iz B ek’s Hegelianism, the subject achieves its autonomy by retroactively

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,380.1,398.7,388.1 role=paragraph -->
positing/reintegrating its own contingent material determinants: freedom is

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,370.1,406.7,378.1 role=paragraph -->
the subjective necessity of objective contingency. But by dissolving the idea

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,360.1,406.7,368.1 role=paragraph -->
of a necessary connection between cause and effect, Meillassoux’s absoluti-

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,350.1,402.7,358.1 role=paragraph -->
zation of contingency not only destroys materialist ‘determinism’ under-

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,340.1,382.7,348.1 role=paragraph -->
stood as the exceptionless continuity of the causal nexus, but also the

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,330.1,394.7,338.1 role=paragraph -->
idealist conception of subjective ‘freedom’ understood in terms of the

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,320.1,378.9,330.6 role=paragraph -->
second-order reflexive causality described by Z B iz B ek. The subject cannot

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,310.1,394.7,318.1 role=paragraph -->
‘choose’ or determine its own objective determination when the contin-

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,300.1,398.7,308.1 role=paragraph -->
gency of all determination implies the equal arbitrariness of every choice,

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,290.1,406.7,298.1 role=paragraph -->
effectively erasing the distinction between forced and un-forced choice. Thus

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,280.1,374.7,288.1 role=paragraph -->
it becomes impossible to distinguish between objective compulsion and

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,270.1,362.7,278.1 role=paragraph -->
subjective reflexion, phenomenal heteronomy and noumenal autonomy.

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,260.1,410.7,268.1 role=paragraph -->
The principle of factuality collapses the distinction between first and second

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,250.1,390.7,258.1 role=paragraph -->
order levels of determination, thereby undermining any attempt to distin-

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=82.7,230.1,410.7,248.1 role=paragraph -->
guish between objective heteronomy and subjective autonomy.    16. ‘Nothingness’ is here understood as the simple negation of all determinate

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,220.1,418.7,228.1 role=paragraph -->
existence. We shall see in Chapters 5 and 6 that there is an alternative defini-

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,210.1,410.7,218.1 role=paragraph -->
tion of nothingness in terms of the determinate identity of ‘being-nothing’

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,200.1,334.7,208.1 role=paragraph -->
and we will try to show why this is perfectly conceivable.

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=82.7,190.1,170.7,198.1 role=citation -->
- 17. Cf. M. Heidegger,

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=82.7,190.1,198.7,198.1 role=paragraph -->
Introduction to Metaphysics , tr. G. Fried & R. Polt, New

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,180.1,258.7,188.1 role=paragraph -->
Haven: Yale University Press, 2000; and The Principle of Reason , tr. R. Lilly,

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,170.1,342.7,178.1 role=paragraph -->
Bloomington and Indianapolis: Indiana University Press, 1996.

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=82.7,160.1,166.7,168.1 role=citation -->
- 18. L. Wittgenstein,

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=82.7,160.1,408.4,168.1 role=paragraph -->
Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus , tr. D. F. Pears & B. F. McGuinness,

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,150.1,194.7,158.1 role=paragraph -->
London: Routledge, 1974.

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=82.7,140.1,150.7,148.1 role=citation -->
- 19. Cf. D. Hume,

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=82.7,140.1,380.4,148.1 role=paragraph -->
A Treatise of Human Nature , ed. C. Mossner, Harmondsworth:

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,130.1,270.7,138.1 role=paragraph -->
Penguin, 1984, Book I, Part III, 117–229.

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=82.7,120.1,406.7,128.1 role=citation -->
- 20. Though Meillassoux states unequivocally that Popper’s anti-inductivism con-

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=98.7,110.1,166.7,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- tinues to assume -->

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=163.2,110.1,407.2,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- the principle of the uniformity of nature, this is debatable. -->

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=341.5,614.1,361.5,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=262 bbox=368.9,614.1,380.9,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 247 -->

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,590.1,402.2,598.1 role=paragraph -->
In fact, Popper’s position is far more nuanced than Meillassoux makes out.

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,580.1,394.2,588.1 role=paragraph -->
Popper seems to distinguish between the metaphysical interpretation of the

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,570.1,414.2,578.1 role=paragraph -->
principle as a thesis about reality, which is unfalsifiable, and its scientific

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,560.1,386.2,568.1 role=paragraph -->
function as a methodological rule which makes no substantial assumptions

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,550.1,380.1,558.1 role=paragraph -->
about the nature of reality. Thus in The Logic of Scientific Discovery Popper

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,540.1,130.2,548.1 role=paragraph -->
writes:

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,518.1,378.2,526.1 role=paragraph -->
Consistently with my attitude toward other metaphysical questions, I

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,508.1,426.2,516.1 role=paragraph -->
abstain from arguing for or against faith in the existence of regularities […]

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,498.1,410.2,506.1 role=paragraph -->
This principle [of the uniformity of nature], it seems to me, expresses in a

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,488.1,378.2,496.1 role=paragraph -->
very superficial way an important methodological rule, and one which

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,478.1,390.2,486.1 role=paragraph -->
might be derived, with advantage, precisely from a consideration of the

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,468.1,426.2,476.1 role=paragraph -->
non-verifiability of theories. […] I think […] that it would be a mistake to

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,458.1,390.2,466.1 role=paragraph -->
assert that natural regularities do not change (This would be a kind of

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,448.1,382.2,456.1 role=paragraph -->
statement that can neither be argued against nor argued for.) What we

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,438.1,388.0,446.1 role=paragraph -->
should say is, rather, that it is part of our definition of natural laws if we

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,428.1,390.2,436.1 role=paragraph -->
postulate that they are to be invariant with respect to space and time;

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,418.1,390.2,426.1 role=paragraph -->
and also if we postulate that they are to have no exceptions. Thus from

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,408.1,394.2,416.1 role=paragraph -->
a methodological point of view, the possibility of falsifying a corrobo-

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,398.1,386.2,406.1 role=paragraph -->
rated law is by no means without significance. It helps us to find out

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,388.1,378.2,396.1 role=paragraph -->
what we demand and expect from natural laws. And the ‘principle of

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,378.1,402.2,386.1 role=paragraph -->
the uniformity of nature’ can again be regarded as a metaphysical inter-

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,368.1,406.2,376.1 role=paragraph -->
pretation of a methodological rule – like its near relative the ‘law of

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,358.1,158.2,366.1 role=paragraph -->
causality’.

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=298.3,348.1,390.3,356.1 role=paragraph -->
(Popper 2002a: 250–1)

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,326.1,390.2,334.1 role=paragraph -->
Elsewhere, Popper seems to reject the principle on the grounds that it is

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,316.1,391.4,324.1 role=paragraph -->
identical with the principle of induction. Thus in Conjectures and Refutations ,

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,306.1,142.2,314.1 role=paragraph -->
he writes:

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,284.1,394.2,292.1 role=paragraph -->
[T]here is a third way of violating the principle of empiricism. We have

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,274.1,390.2,282.1 role=paragraph -->
seen how it can be violated by constructing a theory of knowledge which

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,264.1,406.2,272.1 role=paragraph -->
cannot do without a principle of induction – a principle that tells us in

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,254.1,414.2,262.1 role=paragraph -->
effect that the world is (or very probably is) a place in which men can learn

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,244.1,394.2,252.1 role=paragraph -->
from experience; and that it will remain (or very probably remain) so in

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=106.2,234.1,134.2,242.1 role=paragraph -->
future.

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=306.3,224.1,382.3,232.1 role=paragraph -->
(Popper 2002b: 394)

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,202.1,394.2,210.1 role=paragraph -->
A world in which learning is possible is a uniform world; where uniformity

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,192.1,402.2,200.1 role=paragraph -->
defaults, learning, and a fortiori science, becomes impossible. Yet far from

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,182.1,410.2,190.1 role=paragraph -->
ruling it out, this is precisely a possibility which Popper claims rationalism

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,172.1,382.2,180.1 role=paragraph -->
must embrace. Thus David Miller strongly denies that Popperian critical

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,162.1,386.2,170.1 role=paragraph -->
rationalism is committed to any metaphysical version of the principle of

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,152.1,414.2,160.1 role=paragraph -->
uniformity: ‘Science does not presuppose uniformity, it proposes it, and then

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,142.1,434.2,150.1 role=paragraph -->
attempts to dispose of its proposals’ (Miller 2004: §1). Whether this distinction

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,132.1,390.2,140.1 role=paragraph -->
between scientific proposing and metaphysical presupposing is viable is a

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,122.1,394.2,130.1 role=paragraph -->
complex issue which I shall not pursue further here. But cf. Miller, 1994:

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=98.2,112.1,282.2,120.1 role=paragraph -->
Ch. 2, §2a and Miller 2006: esp. Ch. 4, §3.

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=82.2,614.1,94.2,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 248 -->

<!-- goidev:page=263 bbox=103.9,614.1,123.9,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=82.6,590.1,398.6,598.1 role=citation -->
- 21. Meillassoux borrows this reconstruction of the frequentialist argument from

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,580.1,390.6,588.1 role=paragraph -->
Jean-René Vernes’s Critique de la raison aléatoire, ou Descartes contre Kant , Paris:

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,570.1,378.6,578.1 role=paragraph -->
Aubier, 1982. However, where Vernes endorses the argument, Meillassoux

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,560.1,142.6,568.1 role=paragraph -->
opposes it.

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=82.6,550.1,370.6,558.1 role=citation -->
- 22. This is the upshot of Heidegger’s reinterpretation of Kant in his

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=337.1,550.1,385.1,558.1 role=paragraph -->
Kant and the

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,540.1,342.6,548.1 role=paragraph -->
Problem of Metaphysics , tr. R. Taft, Indianapolis: Indiana University Press, 1990.

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=82.6,530.1,386.6,538.1 role=citation -->
- 23. Cf. Meillasoux 2006: 164. Though Meillassoux does not cite him, Bertrand

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,520.1,382.6,528.1 role=paragraph -->
Russell makes precisely the same point in the opening paragraphs of his

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,510.1,247.4,518.1 role=paragraph -->
Human Knowledge: Its Scope and Limits :

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,488.1,398.6,496.1 role=paragraph -->
Ever since Kant […] there has been what I regard as a mistaken tendency

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,478.1,398.6,486.1 role=paragraph -->
among philosophers to allow the description of the world to be influenced

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,468.1,378.6,476.1 role=paragraph -->
unduly by considerations derived from the nature of human knowledge.

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,458.1,406.6,466.1 role=paragraph -->
To scientific common sense (which I accept) it is plain that only an infin-

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,448.1,406.6,456.1 role=paragraph -->
itesimal part of the universe is known, that there were countless ages dur-

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,438.1,366.6,446.1 role=paragraph -->
ing which there was no knowledge, and that there probably will be

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,428.1,394.6,436.1 role=paragraph -->
countless ages without knowledge in the future. Cosmically and causally,

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,418.1,378.6,426.1 role=paragraph -->
knowledge is an unimportant feature of the universe; a science which

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,408.1,378.6,416.1 role=paragraph -->
omitted to mention its occurrence might, from an impersonal point of

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,398.1,410.6,406.1 role=paragraph -->
view, suffer only a very trivial imperfection. In describing the world, sub-

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,388.1,414.6,396.1 role=paragraph -->
jectivity is a vice. Kant spoke of himself as having effected a ‘Copernican

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,378.1,398.6,386.1 role=paragraph -->
revolution’, but he would have been more accurate if he had spoken of a

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,368.1,410.6,376.1 role=paragraph -->
‘Ptolemaic counter-revolution’, since he put Man back at the centre from

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=106.6,358.1,246.6,366.1 role=paragraph -->
which Copernicus had dethroned him.

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=321.3,348.1,389.3,356.1 role=paragraph -->
(Russell 1948: 9)

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=82.6,326.1,422.6,334.1 role=citation -->
- 24. I have substituted ‘ancestral phenomenon’ for ‘accretion’ in the original

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,316.1,382.6,324.1 role=paragraph -->
passage. Since the accretion of the earth is obviously an example of an

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,306.1,390.6,314.1 role=paragraph -->
ancestral phenomenon, this substitution is intended to clarify the philo-

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,296.1,398.6,304.1 role=paragraph -->
sophical import of the passage without overly distorting the meaning of the

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,286.1,154.6,294.1 role=paragraph -->
original text.

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=82.6,276.1,338.6,284.1 role=citation -->
- 25. Cf. Hilary Putnam’s ‘The Meaning of “Meaning”’ in

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=82.6,276.1,158.6,284.1 role=paragraph -->
Mind, Language, and

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,266.1,250.6,274.1 role=paragraph -->
Reality: Philosophical Papers Volume 2 , Cambridge: Cambridge University

<!-- goidev:page=264 bbox=98.6,256.1,166.6,264.1 role=paragraph -->
Press, 1975, 236.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=86.7,590.1,266.7,598.1 role=citation -->
- 4. This is a point made by Peter Hallward in

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=278.0,590.1,383.9,598.1 role=paragraph -->
Badiou: A Subject to Truth ,

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,580.1,314.2,588.1 role=paragraph -->
Minneapolis: University of Minnesota Press, 2003, 276.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=86.7,570.1,146.7,578.1 role=citation -->
- 5. Cf. Badiou,

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=142.5,570.1,418.6,578.1 role=paragraph -->
Being and Event , Meditation 7. This ‘greater than’ is to be under-

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,560.1,410.2,568.1 role=paragraph -->
stood in terms of the concept of quantity, which is defined in terms of cardi-

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,550.1,406.2,558.1 role=paragraph -->
nality. Badiou establishes the concept of cardinality and the immeasurability

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,540.1,410.2,548.1 role=paragraph -->
of the excess of inclusion over belonging with reference to the Cohen–Easton

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,530.1,391.1,538.1 role=paragraph -->
theorem in Meditation 26 of Being and Event (Badiou 1988: 293–309, 2006a:

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,520.1,150.2,528.1 role=paragraph -->
265–280).

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=86.7,510.1,410.7,518.1 role=citation -->
- 6. For Badiou, ontology is necessarily indifferent to spatio-temporal categories:

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,500.1,414.2,508.1 role=paragraph -->
being qua being has nothing to do with space and/or time – this is of a piece

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,490.1,174.2,498.1 role=paragraph -->
with his Platonism.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=86.7,480.1,402.7,488.1 role=citation -->
- 7. The void only becomes discernible within a situation as a result of the dys-

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,470.1,426.2,478.1 role=paragraph -->
functioning of the count which gives rise to the ‘ultra-one’ of the event; cf.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,460.1,194.2,468.1 role=paragraph -->
Being and Event , Meditations 17 and 18.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=86.7,450.1,146.7,458.1 role=citation -->
- 8. Cf. Badiou,

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=142.5,450.1,202.5,458.1 role=paragraph -->
Being and Event , Meditation 3.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=86.7,440.1,410.7,448.1 role=citation -->
- 9. Cf. Wahl’s incisive paper ‘Presentation, Representation, Appearance’ in

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=363.1,440.1,383.1,448.1 role=paragraph -->
Alain

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,430.1,386.3,438.1 role=paragraph -->
Badiou. Penser le multiple ed. Charles Ramond, Paris: L’Harmattan, 2002,

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,420.1,134.2,428.1 role=paragraph -->
169–87.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=82.2,410.1,382.2,418.1 role=citation -->
- 10. These metaontological concepts must be distinguished from the metaonto-

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,400.1,438.2,408.1 role=paragraph -->
logical use of the term ‘being’, which Badiou is careful not to reify into a con-

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,390.1,430.2,398.1 role=paragraph -->
cept. ‘Being’ is simply a proper name – that of the empty set, Ø – for the

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,380.1,154.2,388.1 role=paragraph -->
unpresentable.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=82.2,370.1,146.2,378.1 role=citation -->
- 11. Cf. Badiou,

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=142.5,370.1,269.1,378.1 role=paragraph -->
Manifeste pour la philosophie , 69.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=82.2,360.1,310.2,368.1 role=citation -->
- 12. For the distinction between truth and knowledge, cf.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=318.7,360.1,378.7,368.1 role=paragraph -->
Being and Event ,

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,350.1,422.2,358.1 role=paragraph -->
Meditations 31 to 36, and also ‘On Subtraction’ and ‘Truth: Forcing and the

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,340.1,240.7,348.1 role=paragraph -->
