<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=82.2,330.1,146.2,338.1 role=citation -->
- 13. Cf. Badiou,

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=142.5,330.1,206.5,338.1 role=paragraph -->
Being and Event , Meditation 16.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=82.2,320.1,146.2,328.1 role=citation -->
- 14. Cf. Badiou,

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=142.5,320.1,206.5,328.1 role=paragraph -->
Being and Event , Meditation 35.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=82.2,310.1,314.2,318.1 role=citation -->
- 15. For Badiou’s account of the impasse of ontology cf.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=82.2,310.1,142.2,318.1 role=paragraph -->
Being and Event ,

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,300.1,394.2,308.1 role=paragraph -->
Meditation 26. Badiou seems to disavow the appeal to transcendence insofar

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,290.1,402.2,298.1 role=paragraph -->
as he aligns it with the onto-theological orientation which he sees exempli-

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,280.1,434.2,288.1 role=paragraph -->
fied in the theory of ‘large cardinals’. A large cardinal is one whose existence

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,270.1,402.2,278.1 role=paragraph -->
is not deducible from the axioms of set-theory and hence requires the asser-

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,260.1,374.2,268.1 role=paragraph -->
tion of a supplementary axiom. Such an axiom is stronger than the one

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,250.1,382.2,258.1 role=paragraph -->
which guarantees the existence of a limit-ordinal and the succession of

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,240.1,414.2,248.1 role=paragraph -->
transfinite alephs. The theory of large cardinals fends off the measurelessness

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,230.1,402.2,238.1 role=paragraph -->
of ontological excess by positing the existence of super-alephs that circum-

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,220.1,434.2,228.1 role=paragraph -->
scribe it ‘from above’. But it is necessary to distinguish between the assertion

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,210.1,402.2,218.1 role=paragraph -->
of the existence of transcendent objects, such as the super-alephs, which do

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,200.1,398.2,208.1 role=paragraph -->
not acknowledge the impasse of ontology, since they do not force a decision

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,190.1,406.2,198.1 role=paragraph -->
as to the value of the power-set of the smallest denumerable infinity, aleph-

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,180.1,402.2,188.1 role=paragraph -->
null; and the assertion of unobjectifiable transcendence, which is precisely

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,170.1,398.2,178.1 role=paragraph -->
the transcendence of decision, i.e. subjective intervention, vis-à-vis the

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,160.1,398.2,168.1 role=paragraph -->
immanent objective parameters of ontological discourse. It is insofar as he

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,150.1,390.2,158.1 role=paragraph -->
endorses the latter option that Badiou can be described as an advocate of

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,140.1,198.2,148.1 role=paragraph -->
radical transcendence.

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=82.2,130.1,414.2,138.1 role=citation -->
- 16. This is the crux of the distinction between natural situations, which are char-

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,120.1,396.2,128.1 role=paragraph -->
acterized by the maximal equilibrium between presentation and re-presentation

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=98.2,110.1,410.2,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- and circumscribed by aleph-null, smallest denumerable infinity; and historical -->

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=82.2,614.1,94.2,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 250 -->

<!-- goidev:page=265 bbox=104.0,614.1,124.0,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,590.1,402.6,598.1 role=paragraph -->
situations, for which that transitivity no longer obtains since they harbour

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,580.1,422.6,588.1 role=paragraph -->
singularities (evental sites) which are potential loci for the singularization of

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,570.1,190.6,578.1 role=paragraph -->
excess. Cf. Being and Event , Meditations 11–16.

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=82.6,560.1,142.6,568.1 role=citation -->
- 17. A. Badiou,

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=82.6,560.1,382.6,568.1 role=paragraph -->
Le Concept de modèle. Introduction a une épistémologie matérialiste des

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,550.1,370.6,558.1 role=paragraph -->
mathématiques , Paris: Maspero, 1969. I have discussed the latter in ‘Badiou’s

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,540.1,406.5,548.1 role=paragraph -->
Materialist Epistemology of Mathematics’ in Angelaki: Journal of the Theoretical

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,530.1,258.6,538.1 role=paragraph -->
Humanities , Vol. 10, No. 2, August 2005, 135–49.

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=82.6,520.1,390.6,528.1 role=citation -->
- 18. This is François Wahl’s recommendation. He argues that Badiou fails to

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,510.1,394.6,518.1 role=paragraph -->
establish a necessary link between the inconsistency of being and the con-

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,500.1,386.6,508.1 role=paragraph -->
sistency of presentation and concludes that subtractive ontology remains

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,490.1,410.6,498.1 role=paragraph -->
insufficient. Thus, he suggests ‘the ontology of presentation and of beings,

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,480.1,390.6,488.1 role=paragraph -->
the ontology of the multiple determinations of being, still remains to be

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,470.1,198.6,478.1 role=paragraph -->
done’ (Wahl 2002: 187).

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=82.6,460.1,142.6,468.1 role=citation -->
- 19. A. Badiou,

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=139.7,460.1,335.7,468.1 role=paragraph -->
Logiques des mondes. L’être et l’événement , 2, Paris: Seuil, 2006.

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=82.6,429.1,157.6,439.1 role=paragraph -->
5 Being Nothing

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=87.2,410.1,295.2,418.1 role=citation -->
- 1. For a detailed bibliography see Laruelle 2003 in

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=282.3,410.1,394.3,418.1 role=paragraph -->
Angelaki, Vol. 8, No. 2: The

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=87.2,390.1,415.2,408.1 role=paragraph -->
One or the Other: French Philosophy Today , August 2003, 188–9.  2. ‘The Transcendental Method’ is an article which Laruelle contributed to the

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,380.1,404.4,388.1 role=paragraph -->
Universal Philosophical Encyclopedia Vol. 1, ed. André Jacob, Paris: PUF, 1989b,

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,370.1,130.6,378.1 role=paragraph -->
71–80.

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=87.2,360.1,111.2,368.1 role=citation -->
- 3. In

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=109.0,360.1,389.0,368.1 role=paragraph -->
Angelaki, Vol. 8, No. 2: The One or the Other: French Philosophy Today , 2003,

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,350.1,134.6,358.1 role=paragraph -->
173–88.

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=87.2,330.1,227.2,348.1 role=citation -->
- 4. Cf. for instance Laruelle 1989a. 5. Cf. particularly

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=156.9,330.1,402.9,338.1 role=paragraph -->
Philosophy and Non-philosophy . I have tried to provide a fuller

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,320.1,370.6,328.1 role=paragraph -->
(and uncritical) account of the scope and remit of non-philosophy as

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,310.1,386.6,318.1 role=paragraph -->
Laruelle sees it in ‘Axiomatic Heresy: The Non-Philosophy of François

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,300.1,355.8,308.1 role=paragraph -->
Laruelle’, Radical Philosophy 121, September/October 2003, 24–35.

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=87.2,290.1,407.2,298.1 role=citation -->
- 6. See for instance Laruelle 1986, Ch. VII, 213–40. As an assiduous student of

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,280.1,398.6,288.1 role=paragraph -->
Heidegger and Derrida, Laruelle is careful to avoid casual uses of the term

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,270.1,450.6,278.1 role=paragraph -->
‘essence’ (unless it is to speak of an ‘essence-without-essence’), preferring to

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,260.1,438.6,268.1 role=paragraph -->
talk of the ‘identity’ of philosophy instead. But what he calls ‘identity’ or

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,250.1,394.6,258.1 role=paragraph -->
‘radical immanence’ amounts to a non-metaphysical conceptualization of

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,240.1,406.6,248.1 role=paragraph -->
essence which, for present purposes, retains most of the characteristic func-

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,230.1,422.6,238.1 role=paragraph -->
tional features associated with the concept of ‘essence’ in its philosophical

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,220.1,410.6,228.1 role=paragraph -->
acceptation. Thus when Laruelle speaks of the ‘identity’ of philosophy, he

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,210.1,382.6,218.1 role=paragraph -->
has in mind something which is a formal invariant, a necessary but non-

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,200.1,310.6,208.1 role=paragraph -->
sufficient condition, and multiply instantiable.

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=87.2,190.1,235.2,198.1 role=citation -->
- 7. Graham Harman has argued that the

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=238.4,190.1,386.1,198.1 role=paragraph -->
Vorhandenheit / Zuzandenheit distinction

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,180.1,398.6,188.1 role=paragraph -->
not only provides the key to understanding the ontic-ontological difference

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,170.1,357.2,178.1 role=paragraph -->
in Being and Time , but ultimately underlies all of Heidegger’s thinking, par-

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,160.1,270.6,168.1 role=paragraph -->
ticularly his critique of metaphysics. Cf. Tool-Being: Heidegger and the

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,150.1,210.6,158.1 role=paragraph -->
Metaphysics of Objects , Chicago: Open Court, 2002.

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=87.2,130.1,387.2,148.1 role=citation -->
- 8. Cf. Laruelle 1986 and 1989, 104–9. 9. Though this reading is supplemented and informed by parallel readings of

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,120.1,414.6,128.1 role=paragraph -->
Plato, Kant, Hegel, Nietzsche, Husserl, Deleuze, and Michel Henry, Laruelle’s

<!-- goidev:page=266 bbox=98.6,110.1,422.6,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- identification of philosophy as ‘decision’ is nevertheless primarily indebted -->

<!-- 251 -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,590.1,406.2,598.1 role=paragraph -->
to Heidegger and Derrida, in whom one can already discern the former’s pre-

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,580.1,398.2,588.1 role=paragraph -->
occupation with uncovering the conditions of reality for conditions of pos-

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,570.1,341.7,578.1 role=paragraph -->
sibility, and in whose work the notions of ‘decision’ ( Entscheidung ) and of the

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,560.1,418.2,568.1 role=paragraph -->
‘undecidable’ first become privileged as clues to the essence of philosophy.

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=82.2,530.1,430.2,558.1 role=citation -->
- 10. Cf. Kant 1929: B33–B116, 65–119. 11. Cf. Kant 1929: A95–A130 and B129–B169, 129–75. 12. Kant 1929: A84–A130, B116–B169, 120–75. Laruelle’s account, indebted to his

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,520.1,392.7,528.1 role=paragraph -->
reading of neo-Kantianism, and particularly to Hermann Cohen’s Kant’s

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,510.1,371.2,518.1 role=paragraph -->
Theory of Experience (2nd edn, 1885), which puts the onus of the first Critique

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,500.1,406.2,508.1 role=paragraph -->
squarely on the principles of the pure understanding, provides an interesting

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,490.1,418.2,498.1 role=paragraph -->
contrast to certain influential interpretations of Kant which locate the essence

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,480.1,378.2,488.1 role=paragraph -->
of transcendental synthesis in the schematism of the imagination, most

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,470.1,387.8,478.1 role=paragraph -->
famously Heidegger (in Kant and the Problem of Metaphysics , Indianapolis:

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,460.1,402.2,468.1 role=paragraph -->
Indiana University Press, 1990a), whose interpretation of Kant was developed

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,450.1,354.2,458.1 role=paragraph -->
in explicit opposition to that of Cohen and the Marburg School.

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=82.2,440.1,110.2,448.1 role=citation -->
- 13. In

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=108.3,440.1,402.1,448.1 role=paragraph -->
The Philosophies of Difference Laruelle explicitly identifies the Heideggerian

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,430.1,409.7,438.1 role=paragraph -->
shift from being as ontic-ontological Differenz to the ‘event of appropriation’

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,420.1,390.9,428.1 role=paragraph -->
( Ereignis ) as Unterschied with the decisional transition from metaphysical to

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,410.1,402.2,418.1 role=paragraph -->
transcendental difference (see Laruelle 1986: 48–120). On the Heideggerian

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,400.1,258.2,408.1 role=paragraph -->
notion of ‘the turning’ ( die Kehre ) see, for example, M. Heidegger, ‘The

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,390.1,302.2,398.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,380.1,370.2,388.1 role=paragraph -->
New York: Harper and Row, 1977a, 36–49; and §255 in Heidegger’s

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,370.1,389.2,378.1 role=paragraph -->
Contributions to Philosophy , tr. P. Emad and K. Maly, Indianapolis: Indiana

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,360.1,226.2,368.1 role=paragraph -->
University Press, 1999, 286–8.

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=82.2,350.1,226.2,358.1 role=citation -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=229.6,350.1,381.6,358.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,330.1,390.7,338.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=82.2,280.1,406.2,288.1 role=citation -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,270.1,410.2,278.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,250.1,462.2,258.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=244.9,240.1,364.9,248.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=82.2,230.1,150.2,238.1 role=citation -->
- 18. F. Laruelle,

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=142.1,230.1,384.1,238.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,220.1,315.9,228.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=82.2,210.1,210.2,218.1 role=citation -->
- 19. Thus in his book on ethics (

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=82.2,210.1,366.1,218.1 role=paragraph -->
Éthique de l’étranger [ Ethics of the Stranger ], Paris:

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,200.1,402.2,208.1 role=paragraph -->
Kimé, 2000b) Laruelle does not actually provide anything like a substantive

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,190.1,398.2,198.1 role=paragraph -->
conceptual analysis of ethical tropes in contemporary philosophy; he simply

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,180.1,378.2,188.1 role=paragraph -->
uses potted versions of Plato, Kant, and Levinas to sketch what a non-

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,170.1,402.2,178.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,160.1,385.9,168.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,150.1,398.2,158.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,140.1,394.2,148.1 role=paragraph -->
readings of Marx, those of Althusser and Henry, as the basis for outlining

<!-- goidev:page=267 bbox=98.2,130.1,334.2,138.1 role=paragraph -->
what a non-philosophical theory of Marxism would look like.

- 21. Laruelle defends this privileging of the ‘name-of-man’ over other nomina-

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,580.1,275.3,588.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=82.6,570.1,434.6,578.1 role=citation -->
- 22. This indifference is largely feigned, as evinced by Laruelle’s occasional displays

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,560.1,382.6,568.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,550.1,426.6,558.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,530.1,410.6,538.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,520.1,389.0,528.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,510.1,414.6,518.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,500.1,381.6,508.1 role=paragraph -->
non-philosophie des contemporains [ The Non-Philosophy of Contemporaries ], Non-

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,490.1,413.1,498.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,480.1,394.6,488.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,470.1,398.6,478.1 role=paragraph -->
Comparison’, 37–46 (written under the pseudonym ‘Tristan Aguilar’).

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=82.6,439.1,252.6,449.1 role=paragraph -->
6 The Pure and Empty Form of Death

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,420.1,171.2,428.1 role=citation -->
- 1. Martin Heidegger,

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,420.1,377.6,428.1 role=paragraph -->
The Concept of Time tr. William McNeill, Oxford:

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,410.1,162.6,418.1 role=paragraph -->
Blackwell, 1992.

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,400.1,243.2,408.1 role=citation -->
- 2. Gadamer, quoted by Theodore Kisiel,

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=247.0,400.1,399.0,408.1 role=paragraph -->
The Genesis of Heidegger’s Being and

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,390.1,310.6,398.1 role=paragraph -->
Time , London: University of California Press, 1993, 315.

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,380.1,171.2,388.1 role=citation -->
- 3. Martin Heidegger,

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,380.1,263.2,388.1 role=paragraph -->
Being and Time , tr. J. Macquarrie and E. Robinson, Oxford:

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,370.1,166.6,378.1 role=paragraph -->
Blackwell, 1962.

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,360.1,179.2,368.1 role=citation -->
- 4. Kisiel 1993: 23–5.

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,350.1,351.2,358.1 role=paragraph -->
5. ‘ Augenblick ’ is of course Nietzsche’s term to describe the moment wherein

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,340.1,406.6,348.1 role=paragraph -->
eternal recurrence is confronted. We shall discuss Deleuze’s account of the

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,330.1,394.6,338.1 role=paragraph -->
relation between eternal recurrence and ontological transcendence below.

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,320.1,195.2,328.1 role=citation -->
- 6. Heidegger will contrast

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=188.1,320.1,408.1,328.1 role=paragraph -->
Dasein ’s radically individuated, unobjectifiable ‘self’

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,310.1,394.6,318.1 role=paragraph -->
to the impersonal anonymity of the metaphysical or transcendental subject.

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,300.1,155.2,308.1 role=citation -->
- 7. M. Heidegger,

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,300.1,382.1,308.1 role=paragraph -->
The Basic Problems of Phenomenology , tr. A. Hofstader

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,290.1,334.6,298.1 role=paragraph -->
Bloomington & Indianapolis: Indiana University Press, 1982.

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,280.1,415.2,288.1 role=citation -->
- 8. Cf. Françoise Dastur,‘The Ekstatico-Horizonal Constitution of Temporality’

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,270.1,373.8,278.1 role=paragraph -->
in C. Macann, ed. Critical Heidegger , London: Routledge, 1996, 158–71.

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=87.2,260.1,399.2,268.1 role=citation -->
- 9. No doubt this difficulty had something to do with Heidegger’s abandonment

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,250.1,402.6,258.1 role=paragraph -->
of the project of fundamental ontology. But why does the latter unravel pre-

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,240.1,386.7,248.1 role=paragraph -->
cisely at that point where the existential analytic, outlining Dasein’s ekstatic

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,230.1,390.6,238.1 role=paragraph -->
structure of transcendence, was to be surpassed towards an account of the

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,220.1,414.6,228.1 role=paragraph -->
temporality proper to being as such? Heidegger’s retrieval of the ontological

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,210.1,406.1,218.1 role=paragraph -->
problematic in Being and Time was to be effected via a critical radicalization

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,200.1,398.6,208.1 role=paragraph -->
of transcendental philosophy. The fundamental question is not just of being

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,190.1,390.6,198.1 role=paragraph -->
but of our access to being: how do we originally access the being of phe-

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,180.1,391.6,188.1 role=paragraph -->
nomena? Dasein is in the world but also not just something in the world.

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,170.1,402.6,178.1 role=paragraph -->
Herein lies the rub: where Kantian transcendentalism cultivated suspicion of

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,160.1,362.6,168.1 role=paragraph -->
unmediated access to phenomena, transcendental phenomenology coun-

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,150.1,398.6,158.1 role=paragraph -->
tered with the revelation that the mediation is immediate, i.e. unmediated.

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,140.1,406.6,148.1 role=paragraph -->
That which is accessed is mediated, but the access as such is not, whether it

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,130.1,406.6,138.1 role=paragraph -->
be intentionality or finite transcendence. Finite transcendence is the condi-

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,120.1,426.6,128.1 role=paragraph -->
tion of possibility for all access to the being of phenomena – indeed, according

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=98.6,110.1,410.6,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- to Heidegger, it is the condition of possibility for those merely metaphysical -->

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=341.5,614.1,361.5,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=268 bbox=368.9,614.1,380.9,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 253 -->

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,590.1,426.2,598.1 role=paragraph -->
conditions of possibility identified by the ontotheological tradition – but this

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,580.1,418.2,588.1 role=paragraph -->
condition of conditions is necessarily unconditioned: it is ‘the ekstatikon in

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,570.1,422.2,578.1 role=paragraph -->
and for itself’. Recognizing the taint of idealist subjectivism in this latter,

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,560.1,390.2,568.1 role=paragraph -->
Heidegger went on to seek an even more originary access to the primordial

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,550.1,406.2,558.1 role=paragraph -->
‘happening’, and ever more radical means of unearthing the conditions for

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,540.1,367.2,548.1 role=paragraph -->
conditions: Ereignis , the fourfold, etc. The phenomenological radicalization

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,530.1,394.2,538.1 role=paragraph -->
of transcendentalism initiated by Heidegger finds itself excavating deeper

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,520.1,386.2,528.1 role=paragraph -->
and deeper into the primordial: uncovering the conditions for the condi-

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,510.1,418.2,518.1 role=paragraph -->
tions of the conditions, etc. Yet  the deeper it digs towards the pre-originary,

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,500.1,406.2,508.1 role=paragraph -->
the greater its remove from ‘things themselves’ and the more impoverished

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,490.1,394.2,498.1 role=paragraph -->
its resources become. Heidegger and his successors – up to and including

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,480.1,410.2,488.1 role=paragraph -->
Laruelle – end up burrowing ever deeper into reflexivity in order to unearth

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,470.1,402.2,478.1 role=paragraph -->
the pre-reflexive, exacerbating abstraction until it becomes reduced to ply-

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,460.1,402.2,468.1 role=paragraph -->
ing its own exorbitant vacuity. Derrida introduces both a healthy measure of

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,450.1,398.2,458.1 role=paragraph -->
scepticism and a fatal dose of irony into this meta-transcendental problem-

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,440.1,410.2,448.1 role=paragraph -->
atic by revealing how the immediacy of access was ‘always already’ contam-

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,430.1,383.2,438.1 role=paragraph -->
inated by différance as inclusive disjunction of mediation and immediacy.

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,420.1,398.2,428.1 role=paragraph -->
But he is trumped by Laruelle, who unveils the unobjectifiable immediacy of

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,410.1,414.2,418.1 role=paragraph -->
‘man’ as that which is always already presupposed by and hence the ultimate

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,400.1,385.9,408.1 role=paragraph -->
determinant for différance’s inclusive disjunction of mediation and immedi-

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,390.1,390.2,398.1 role=paragraph -->
acy (cf. Chapter 5). Once the problematic of access, and of the access to

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,380.1,406.2,388.1 role=paragraph -->
access, has reached its absurd denouement in the claim that this ‘man with-

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,370.1,398.2,378.1 role=paragraph -->
out qualities’ is the primal phenomenon determining the conditions of the

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,360.1,418.2,368.1 role=paragraph -->
conditions of access, it is no surprise to see the very notion of a world indif-

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,350.1,410.2,358.1 role=paragraph -->
ferent to our access to it recede into unintelligibility. But if the idea of a

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,340.1,394.2,348.1 role=paragraph -->
world independent of our access to it becomes unintelligible, then perhaps

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,330.1,430.2,338.1 role=paragraph -->
the fault lies with the correlational criteria of intelligibility stipulated by the

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,320.1,394.2,328.1 role=paragraph -->
philosophy of access, rather than with the world. One cannot but be struck

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,310.1,402.2,318.1 role=paragraph -->
by the comic spectacle of the later Heidegger trying to uncover the roots of

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,300.1,234.2,308.1 role=paragraph -->
the primal phenomenon, the Ur-etwas , in old Greek words. The phenome-

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,290.1,418.2,298.1 role=paragraph -->
nology that sought to begin again with ‘the things themselves’ is redirected

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,280.1,370.2,288.1 role=paragraph -->
by Heidegger and ends up poring over words, nothing but words …...

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,270.1,354.2,278.1 role=paragraph -->
Perhaps this is the inevitable fate of the philosophy of access.

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=82.2,260.1,406.2,268.1 role=citation -->
- 10. This disjunction will provide Heidegger’s heirs, such as Levinas, Blanchot,

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,250.1,406.2,258.1 role=paragraph -->
and Derrida, with the ever-popular theme of ‘the impossibility of death’.

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=82.2,240.1,170.2,248.1 role=citation -->
- 11. Cf. M. Heidegger,

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=82.2,240.1,310.2,248.1 role=paragraph -->
The Fundamental Problems of Metaphysics: World, Finitude,

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,230.1,354.2,238.1 role=paragraph -->
Solitude , tr. W. McNeill, Bloomington & Indianapolis: Indiana University

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,220.1,406.2,228.1 role=paragraph -->
Press, 1995. Heidegger’s attempt to wriggle out of this dichotomy by claim-

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,210.1,406.2,218.1 role=paragraph -->
ing that the distinction at issue is not between having or not having a world

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,200.1,418.2,208.1 role=paragraph -->
but rather between entities that are ‘rich in world’ (i.e. human beings) and

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,190.1,422.2,198.1 role=paragraph -->
those that are ‘poor in world’ (such as animals) is a desperate sophism since

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,180.1,398.2,188.1 role=paragraph -->
he makes it perfectly clear that there can be no common measure for degrees

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,170.1,438.2,178.1 role=paragraph -->
of ‘richness’ or ‘poverty’ in world and hence no possible transition from one

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,160.1,410.2,168.1 role=paragraph -->
to the other. The fact that such transitions frequently occur within the realm

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,150.1,411.3,158.1 role=paragraph -->
of Dasein – e.g. in cases of brain-damage or dementia – only underlines the

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,140.1,298.2,148.1 role=paragraph -->
explanatory poverty of Heidegger’s distinction.

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=82.2,130.1,402.2,138.1 role=citation -->
- 12. It is important to register the way in which Heidegger’s ontologization of

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,120.1,442.2,128.1 role=paragraph -->
Dasein ’s ‘historicality’ licenses a total disregard for the merely ‘ontic’ details

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,110.1,110.2,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- of -->

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=107.8,110.1,131.8,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- Dasein -->

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=107.8,110.1,411.8,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- ’s empirical and/or natural history – and it is precisely this disregard -->

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=98.2,100.1,406.2,108.1 role=footer -->
<!-- which will provide the precondition for Heidegger’s ‘history of being’. -->

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=82.2,614.1,94.2,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 254 -->

<!-- goidev:page=269 bbox=104.0,614.1,124.0,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=82.6,590.1,146.6,598.1 role=citation -->
- 13. G. Deleuze,

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=82.6,590.1,392.6,598.1 role=paragraph -->
Différence et répétition , Paris: PUF, 1968; Difference and Repetition ,

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,580.1,334.6,588.1 role=paragraph -->
tr. Paul Patton, New York: Columbia University Press, 1994.

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=82.6,570.1,426.6,578.1 role=citation -->
- 14. Already in 1956’s ‘Bergson’s Conception of Difference’, Deleuze is arguing

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,560.1,410.6,568.1 role=paragraph -->
that to conceive of being as pure self-differentiation is to conceive of it in

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,550.1,406.6,558.1 role=paragraph -->
Bergsonian terms as duration: ‘Duration, tendency, is self-differentiating;

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,540.1,385.2,548.1 role=paragraph -->
and what differs from itself is immediately the unity of substance and sub-

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,530.1,411.1,538.1 role=paragraph -->
ject’. G. Deleuze, ‘La conception de la différence chez Bergson’ in L’île déserte

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,520.1,306.7,528.1 role=paragraph -->
et autres textes , Paris: Minuit, 2002a, 52. As we shall see, though Difference and

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,510.1,415.4,518.1 role=paragraph -->
Repetition will qualify and complicate this claim about the ‘immediate’ unity

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,500.1,398.7,508.1 role=paragraph -->
of substance and subject, or being and thought, in its account of the third

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,490.1,290.7,498.1 role=paragraph -->
synthesis, the re-inscription of Hegel persists.

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=82.7,480.1,402.7,488.1 role=citation -->
- 15. Deleuze attributes the distinction between death as personal possibility and

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,470.1,414.7,478.1 role=paragraph -->
dying as impersonal impossibility of possibility (the death of the Other in me)

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,460.1,390.0,468.1 role=paragraph -->
to Blanchot, citing three works: The Space of Literature (originally published

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,450.1,366.7,458.1 role=paragraph -->
1955; tr. A. Smock, Nebraska: University of Nebraska Press, 1982); The Book to

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,440.1,412.6,448.1 role=paragraph -->
Come (originally published 1959, tr. C. Mandell, Stanford: Stanford University

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,430.1,402.7,438.1 role=paragraph -->
Press, 2002); and ‘The Laughter of the Gods’ (originally published 1965;

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,420.1,372.7,428.1 role=paragraph -->
included in Friendship , tr. E. Rottenberg, Stanford: Stanford University Press,

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,410.1,390.7,418.1 role=paragraph -->
1997). But though Deleuze may not have been aware of it, Blanchot derives

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,400.1,414.7,408.1 role=paragraph -->
this distinction more or less directly from Levinas, whose influence thoroughly

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,390.1,302.7,398.1 role=paragraph -->
pervades Blanchot’s oeuvre . Indeed, it would be difficult to overestimate the

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,380.1,422.7,388.1 role=paragraph -->
influence of Levinas’s key tropes upon Blanchot’s thinking – the impersonal

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,370.1,346.7,378.1 role=paragraph -->
anonymity of the ‘ il y a ’, radical passivity, the Other, etc. Thus Blanchot’s ver-

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,360.1,414.7,368.1 role=paragraph -->
sion of the distinction between death and dying comes from Levinas’s critique

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,350.1,337.0,358.1 role=paragraph -->
of Heidegger in Time and the Other , originally published in 1948 (tr. R. Cohen,

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,340.1,418.7,348.1 role=paragraph -->
Pittsburgh: Dusquesne University Press, 1987). However, Levinas’s inversion of

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,330.1,381.7,338.1 role=paragraph -->
the relation between possibility and impossibility in Being and Time remains

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,320.1,410.6,328.1 role=paragraph -->
entirely dependent upon the conceptual machinery of Heidegger’s text, and it

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,310.1,426.6,318.1 role=paragraph -->
is arguable that far from subverting the latter, this reversibility between possi-

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,300.1,342.6,308.1 role=paragraph -->
bility and impossibility is one of its enabling conditions.

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=82.6,290.1,146.6,298.1 role=citation -->
- 16. H. Bergson,

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=82.6,290.1,391.0,298.1 role=paragraph -->
Matter and Memory , tr. N. M. Paul and W. S. Palmer, New York:

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,280.1,166.6,288.1 role=paragraph -->
Zone Books, 1991.

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=82.6,270.1,426.6,278.1 role=citation -->
- 17. Curiously, this is rendered as ‘Ideas and the Synthesis of Difference’ in Paul

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,260.1,222.6,268.1 role=paragraph -->
Patton’s English translation.

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=82.6,250.1,358.6,258.1 role=citation -->
- 18. Timothy Murphy has pointed out that Deleuze’s contrast between

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=337.3,250.1,401.3,258.1 role=paragraph -->
répétition nue

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,240.1,396.5,248.1 role=paragraph -->
and répétition vétue must be understood in terms of the theatrical metaphorics

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,230.1,384.5,238.1 role=paragraph -->
which run throughout Difference and Repetition . In French, répétition vétue also

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,220.1,426.6,228.1 role=paragraph -->
means ‘dress rehearsal’. Cf. Timothy Murphy, ‘The Theatre of (the Philosophy

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,210.1,385.9,218.1 role=paragraph -->
of) Cruelty in Difference and Repetition ’ in Pli: The Warwick Journal of Philosophy ,

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,200.1,389.4,208.1 role=paragraph -->
Vol. 5 . Deleuze and the Transcendental Unconscious , ed. J. Broadhurst-Dixon,

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,190.1,302.6,198.1 role=paragraph -->
Coventry: University of Warwick, 1992, 105–35.

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=82.6,180.1,230.6,188.1 role=citation -->
- 19. Though Badiou tends to privilege

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=222.4,180.1,397.4,188.1 role=paragraph -->
Difference and Repetition (alongside Deleuze’s

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.6,170.1,393.7,178.1 role=paragraph -->
two Cinema volumes) to the detriment of both volumes of ‘Capitalism and

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,160.1,426.7,168.1 role=paragraph -->
Schizophrenia’ in his critique of Deleuze, he seems to disregard the role of the

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,150.1,406.7,158.1 role=paragraph -->
third synthesis as locus of thought’s conversion from contemplation to pro-

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,140.1,418.7,148.1 role=paragraph -->
duction, which at the very least problematizes his depiction of Deleuze’s phi-

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,130.1,387.3,138.1 role=paragraph -->
losophy as essentially contemplative. Cf. A. Badiou, Deleuze: The Clamour of

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=98.7,120.1,382.7,128.1 role=paragraph -->
Being , tr. L. Burchill, Minneapolis: University of Minnesota Press, 2002.

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=82.7,110.1,354.7,118.1 role=footnote -->
> [^note]: 20. Deleuze’s account is heavily indebted to Gilbert Simondon’s

<!-- goidev:page=270 bbox=328.6,110.1,400.6,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- L’Individu et sa -->

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,590.1,314.2,598.1 role=citation -->
- 21. Cf. G. Deleuze, ‘La méthode de dramatisation’ in

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=285.5,590.1,421.5,598.1 role=paragraph -->
L’île déserte et autres textes ,

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,580.1,222.2,588.1 role=paragraph -->
Paris: Minuit, 2002b, 131–62.

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,570.1,382.2,578.1 role=citation -->
- 22. I owe this crucial insight to Alberto Toscano’s indispensable study,

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=369.0,570.1,381.0,578.1 role=paragraph -->
The

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,560.1,402.2,568.1 role=paragraph -->
Theatre of Production: Philosophy and Individuation between Kant and Deleuze ,

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,550.1,382.2,558.1 role=paragraph -->
Basingstoke: Palgrave Macmillan, 2006. Cf. especially Chapter 6 and the

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,540.1,186.2,548.1 role=paragraph -->
Conclusion, 157–201.

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,530.1,438.2,538.1 role=citation -->
- 23. For accounts of the role of ‘expression’ in Deleuze’s thought which differ from

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,520.1,370.2,528.1 role=paragraph -->
the one presented here, see Len Lawlor, ‘The End of Phenomenology:

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,510.1,391.6,518.1 role=paragraph -->
Expressionism in Deleuze and Merleau-Ponty’ in Continental Philosophy Review ,

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,500.1,406.2,508.1 role=paragraph -->
Vol. 31, No. 1, 1998, 15–34; and Simon Duffy, ‘The Logic of Expression in

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,490.1,279.2,498.1 role=paragraph -->
Deleuze’s Expressionism in Philosophy: Spinoza : A Strategy of Engagement’ in

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,470.1,426.2,488.1 role=paragraph -->
International Journal of Philosophical Studies , Vol. 12, No. 1, 2004, 47–60. 24. ‘We call this dark precursor, this difference in itself or second-degree differ-

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,460.1,422.2,468.1 role=paragraph -->
ence, which relates disparate or heterogeneous series to one another, “the dis-

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,450.1,458.2,458.1 role=paragraph -->
parate”’ (Deleuze 1968: 157, 1994: 120 tm). ‘We call “disparity” this infinitely

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,440.1,430.2,448.1 role=paragraph -->
redoubled, infinitely resonating state of difference. Disparity, i.e. difference or

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,430.1,406.2,438.1 role=paragraph -->
intensity (difference of intensity), is the sufficient reason of the phenome-

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,420.1,414.2,428.1 role=paragraph -->
non, the condition of that which appears. […] The reason of the sensible, the

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,410.1,394.2,418.1 role=paragraph -->
condition of that which appears, is not space and time, but the Unequal in

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,400.1,388.7,408.1 role=paragraph -->
itself, or disparateness such as is comprised and determined in difference of

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,380.1,422.2,398.1 role=paragraph -->
intensity, in intensity as difference’ (Deleuze 1968: 287, 1994: 222–3 tm).  25. ‘A distribution conforms to good sense when it tends by itself to banish dif-

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,370.1,354.2,378.1 role=paragraph -->
ference in the distributed’ (Deleuze 1968: 289, 1994: 224 tm).

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,360.1,402.2,368.1 role=citation -->
- 26. It is this supplementary dimension constituted by reflection and inherent in

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,350.1,386.2,358.1 role=paragraph -->
representation which Deleuze will denounce ever more emphatically in all

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,340.1,402.2,348.1 role=paragraph -->
his subsequent work. Thus his increasing insistence upon the ‘immanence’

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,330.1,390.2,338.1 role=paragraph -->
which is the proper element of philosophical thought, and upon the neces-

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,320.1,402.2,328.1 role=paragraph -->
sity of simultaneously constructing and expressing it, follows directly from

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,310.1,414.2,318.1 role=paragraph -->
this critical circumscription of the secondary and derivative nature of reflex-

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,290.1,410.2,308.1 role=paragraph -->
ive consciousness in Difference and Repetition .  27. ‘Sense is like the Idea which is developed through sub-representative deter-

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,280.1,286.2,288.1 role=paragraph -->
minations’ (Deleuze 1968: 201, 1994: 155 tm).

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,270.1,294.2,278.1 role=citation -->
- 28. Cf. Keith Ansell-Pearson, ‘Dead or Alive’ in

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=280.7,270.1,392.7,278.1 role=paragraph -->
Viroid Life: Perspectives on

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,250.1,430.2,268.1 role=paragraph -->
Nietzsche and the Transhuman Condition , London: Routledge, 1997, 57–83.  29. ‘Every phenomenon flashes forth in a signal-sign system. We call ‘signal’ the

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,240.1,402.2,248.1 role=paragraph -->
system such as it is constituted by or bounded by at least two heterogeneous

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,230.1,406.2,238.1 role=paragraph -->
series; two disparate orders capable of entering into communication; the phe-

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,220.1,410.2,228.1 role=paragraph -->
nomenon is a sign, i.e. that which flashes forth in the system when these dis-

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,210.1,410.2,218.1 role=paragraph -->
parates enter into communication’ (Deleuze 1968: 286–7, 1994: 222 tm). Cf.

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,200.1,398.2,208.1 role=paragraph -->
Daniel W. Smith, ‘Deleuze’s Theory of Sensation: Overcoming the Kantian

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,190.1,406.8,198.1 role=paragraph -->
Duality’ in Deleuze: A Critical Reader , ed. P. Patton, Oxford: Blackwell, 1995, 34.

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,180.1,218.2,188.1 role=citation -->
- 30. Cf. D. Deleuze and C. Parnet,

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,170.1,414.2,188.1 role=paragraph -->
Dialogues , Paris: Flammarion, 1977, 68–72. 31. ‘[P]roblematic Ideas are at once the ultimate elements of nature and the sub-

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,160.1,390.2,168.1 role=paragraph -->
liminal object of small perceptions. Learning always proceeds through the

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,150.1,398.2,158.1 role=paragraph -->
unconscious; it always takes place in the unconscious, thereby establishing

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,140.1,374.2,148.1 role=paragraph -->
the bond of a profound complicity between nature and mind’ (Deleuze

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,120.1,418.2,138.1 role=paragraph -->
1968: 214, 1994: 165 tm). 32. ‘Unlike the physico-chemical sphere, where the “code” that underlies forms

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=98.2,110.1,414.2,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- or qualities is distributed throughout the three-dimensionality of a structure, -->

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=82.2,614.1,94.2,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 256 -->

<!-- goidev:page=271 bbox=103.9,614.1,123.9,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,590.1,366.6,598.1 role=paragraph -->
in the organic sphere this code becomes detached as a separate one-

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,580.1,402.6,588.1 role=paragraph -->
dimensional structure: the linear sequence of nucleic acids constituting the

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,570.1,392.6,578.1 role=paragraph -->
genetic code.’ Manuel De Landa, Intensive Science and Virtual Philosophy ,

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,560.1,394.6,568.1 role=paragraph -->
London: Continuum, 2002, 163–4. While this is in many ways a very useful

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,550.1,426.6,558.1 role=paragraph -->
gloss, the claim that individuating factors constitute a ‘code’ is problematic

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,540.1,386.6,548.1 role=paragraph -->
on two counts. First, it seems to ignore Deleuze’s distinction between

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,530.1,394.6,538.1 role=paragraph -->
individuating and individual differences, which is the distinction between

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,520.1,406.6,528.1 role=paragraph -->
enveloping intensity as clear expression of a distinct difference in the Idea

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,510.1,410.6,518.1 role=paragraph -->
and enveloped intensity as confused expression of the Idea’s obscure perpli-

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,500.1,406.6,508.1 role=paragraph -->
cation: ‘Two individuating intensities may be abstractly the same by virtue

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,490.1,406.6,498.1 role=paragraph -->
of what they clearly express; they are never the same on account of the order

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,480.1,382.6,488.1 role=paragraph -->
of intensities which they envelop or the relations which they obscurely

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,470.1,422.6,478.1 role=paragraph -->
express’ (Deleuze 1968: 326, 1994: 253 tm). This irreducible variability in the

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,460.1,394.6,468.1 role=paragraph -->
correlation between individuating differences and pre-individual singular-

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,450.1,390.6,458.1 role=paragraph -->
ities would seem to indicate an order of complexity which is difficult to

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,440.1,386.6,448.1 role=paragraph -->
codify in an information-theoretic register. Second, it is not clear how

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,430.1,402.6,438.1 role=paragraph -->
individuating factors could become detached as a ‘separate one-dimensional

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,420.1,398.6,428.1 role=paragraph -->
structure’ without themselves becoming individuated. Intensive individua-

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,410.1,426.6,418.1 role=paragraph -->
tion was supposed to provide part of the ‘sufficient reason’ for actualization

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,400.1,402.6,408.1 role=paragraph -->
(Deleuze 1968: 285, 1994: 221), not its cause in extensity, and if the indi-

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,390.1,390.6,398.1 role=paragraph -->
viduating factors invoked in order to account for actualization are them-

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,380.1,398.6,388.1 role=paragraph -->
selves already individuated then the virtual–actual distinction collapses

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=82.6,360.1,426.6,378.1 role=paragraph -->
and an infinite regress looms. 33. ‘“Possible” here is not to be understood as implying resemblance, but rather

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,350.1,410.6,358.1 role=paragraph -->
as the state of the implicated or the enveloped in its heterogeneity with that

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,340.1,322.6,348.1 role=paragraph -->
which envelops it’ (Deleuze 1968: 334, 1994: 260 tm).

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=82.6,330.1,402.6,338.1 role=citation -->
- 34. The proximity to Levinas and Blanchot regarding the theme of ‘the death of

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,320.1,414.6,328.1 role=paragraph -->
the Other’ has already been noted. Where Deleuze’s account differs conspic-

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,310.1,414.6,318.1 role=paragraph -->
uously from both, however is in the notable paucity of references to ‘radical

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,300.1,410.6,308.1 role=paragraph -->
passivity’, a recurring trope whenever Blanchot or Levinas discuss the rela-

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,290.1,382.6,298.1 role=paragraph -->
tion between death and alterity. For Deleuze, by way of contrast, dying

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=82.6,270.1,402.6,288.1 role=paragraph -->
seems rather to be a function of the act of thinking. 35. ‘The indivisibility of the individual pertains exclusively to the property

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,260.1,386.6,268.1 role=paragraph -->
whereby intensive quantities cannot divide without changing in nature’

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,250.1,422.6,258.1 role=paragraph -->
(Deleuze 1968: 327, 1994: 254 tm). The latter is precisely Bergson’s definition

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,240.1,414.6,248.1 role=paragraph -->
of duration as qualitative multiplicity, which he contrasts to the quantitative

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,230.1,226.6,238.1 role=paragraph -->
multiplicities proper to space.

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=82.6,220.1,210.6,228.1 role=citation -->
- 36. Cf. Alfred North Whitehead,

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=82.6,220.1,186.6,228.1 role=paragraph -->
Process and Reality , London and New York: The

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,210.1,410.6,218.1 role=paragraph -->
Free Press, 1978; David Chalmers, ‘Is Experience Ubiquitous?’ in Chapter 8

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,200.1,258.6,208.1 role=paragraph -->
of Chalmers’s The Conscious Mind , Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1996.

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=82.6,190.1,390.6,198.1 role=citation -->
- 37. Thus De Landa (2002), for instance, proposes a reading of Deleuze wherein

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,180.1,402.6,188.1 role=paragraph -->
virtuality becomes the preserve of theoretical entities such as phase spaces

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,170.1,394.6,178.1 role=paragraph -->
and dynamic attractors. But as Alberto Toscano has pointed out, he does so

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,160.1,406.6,168.1 role=paragraph -->
at the cost of eliding Deleuze’s fundamental distinction between virtuality

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=82.6,140.1,422.6,158.1 role=paragraph -->
and possibility. Cf. Toscano (2006), 184–7.  38. ‘Sense is the genesis or production of the true, and truth is merely the empir-

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,130.1,434.6,138.1 role=paragraph -->
ical result of sense. […] Nevertheless, the Idea which traverses all the faculties

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,120.1,422.6,128.1 role=paragraph -->
is not reducible to sense. For it is just as much non-sense; and there is no dif-

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,110.1,358.6,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- ficulty reconciling this double-aspect through which the Idea is -->

<!-- goidev:page=272 bbox=98.6,110.1,142.6,118.1 role=footer -->
<!-- constituted -->

by structural elements which have no sense in themselves, while constitut-

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,580.1,398.2,588.1 role=paragraph -->
ing the sense of everything it produces (structure and genesis)’ (Deleuze

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=82.2,547.1,207.2,578.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=86.7,428.1,142.7,436.1 role=citation -->

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=86.2,408.1,410.2,426.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,388.1,390.2,396.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=86.7,348.1,142.7,356.1 role=citation -->
- 9. Nietzsche,

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=138.9,348.1,254.4,356.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=82.2,338.1,406.2,346.1 role=citation -->
- 10. As is well known, there are only three explicit mentions of ‘eternal recur-

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,328.1,332.0,336.1 role=paragraph -->
rence’ in Nietzsche’s published works: The Gay Science , IV, §341, ‘The

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,318.1,262.2,326.1 role=paragraph -->
Heaviest Burden’; Thus Spake Zarathustra , III, ‘Of the Vision and the Riddle’

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,308.1,313.2,316.1 role=paragraph -->
and ‘The Convalescent’; and Beyond Good and Evil , III, §56. However, some

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,298.1,402.2,306.1 role=paragraph -->
inkling of its importance for Nietzsche is given by the frequency with which

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,288.1,382.3,296.1 role=paragraph -->
it is invoked in his unpublished notebooks. Thus The Will to Power contains

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,278.1,390.2,286.1 role=paragraph -->
not only numerous references but also several explicit discussions of the

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,268.1,398.2,276.1 role=paragraph -->
idea: specifically, in sections 617, 708, 1057, 1058, 1059, 1060, 1062, and

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,258.1,418.2,266.1 role=citation -->
- 1066. Despite their sometimes controversial interpretations, it is to the credit

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,248.1,418.2,256.1 role=paragraph -->
of the ‘strong’ readings of Nietzsche proposed by Heidegger and Deleuze that

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,238.1,398.2,246.1 role=paragraph -->
they position the doctrine of eternal recurrence (along with the concept of

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,228.1,342.2,236.1 role=paragraph -->
will to power) at the very heart of Nietzsche’s philosophy.

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=82.2,208.1,382.2,226.1 role=citation -->
- 11. Nietzsche, 1968: §54, cf. also §708 and §1062. 12. Translated by Hugh Tomlinson, London: Athlone, 1983. Deleuze’s famous

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,198.1,386.2,206.1 role=paragraph -->
(not to say notorious) interpretation of eternal recurrence in this book

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,188.1,409.8,196.1 role=paragraph -->
insists that it is not identity – the world as yoked beneath the iron collar of

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,178.1,414.2,186.1 role=paragraph -->
representation – that returns, but rather difference – the world as dynamic

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,168.1,406.2,176.1 role=paragraph -->
flux of pre-individual singularities and impersonal individuations. The trou-

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,158.1,426.2,166.1 role=paragraph -->
ble with this audacious proposal is that it flies in the face of Nietzsche’s own

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,148.1,414.2,156.1 role=paragraph -->
understanding of the nature of eternal recurrence. Nietzsche insists that it is

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,138.1,390.2,146.1 role=paragraph -->
precisely the moment as apprehended from the perspective of the individu-

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,128.1,394.2,136.1 role=paragraph -->
ated self that will be eternally repeated, not the world as experienced by

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,118.1,394.2,126.1 role=paragraph -->
Deleuze’s anonymous, intensive individual, who cannot be confined by the

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=98.2,108.1,410.2,116.1 role=footer -->
<!-- form of the I or the matter of the self (cf. Chapter 6). Zarathustra could not -->

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=82.2,614.1,94.2,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 258 -->

<!-- goidev:page=273 bbox=103.9,614.1,123.9,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,590.1,438.6,598.1 role=paragraph -->
be more explicit: ‘I shall return, with this sun, with this earth, with this eagle,

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,580.1,430.6,588.1 role=paragraph -->
with this serpent – not to a new life or a better life or a similar life: I shall

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,570.1,434.6,578.1 role=paragraph -->
return eternally to this identical and self-same life, in the greatest things and in

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,560.1,383.7,568.1 role=paragraph -->
the smallest, to teach once more the eternal recurrence of things.’ ( Thus Spake

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,550.1,354.6,558.1 role=paragraph -->
Zarathustra , tr. R. J. Hollingdale, Harmondsworth: Penguin, 1969, 237–8.)

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,540.1,388.9,548.1 role=paragraph -->
Elsewhere Nietzsche explicitly evokes the ‘infinite recurrence of identical

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,530.1,426.6,538.1 role=paragraph -->
cases’ (1968: §1066, emphasis added) – just as he insists that the hypothesis

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,520.1,390.7,528.1 role=paragraph -->
proceeds on the assumption that the world comprises ‘a certain definite quan-

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,510.1,402.0,518.1 role=paragraph -->
tity of force and a certain definite number of certain centers of force’ (ibid.,

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,500.1,414.6,508.1 role=paragraph -->
emphasis added) – in other words, individuated loci of will to power, and not

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,490.1,388.6,498.1 role=paragraph -->
the process of intensive individuation privileged by Deleuze in Difference and

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,480.1,366.6,488.1 role=paragraph -->
Repetition . Here we may detect a tension between Deleuze’s anti-mechanistic

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,470.1,402.6,478.1 role=paragraph -->
conception of will to power in terms of quanta of force which are inherently

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,460.1,394.6,468.1 role=paragraph -->
unequalizable and hence beyond the reach of scientific quantification (cf.

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,450.1,398.6,458.1 role=paragraph -->
Deleuze 1983: 42–6), and Nietzsche’s own blunt avowal that he wishes to

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,440.1,402.6,448.1 role=paragraph -->
reconcile mechanism and Platonism (cf. 1968: §1061) – precisely the arch-

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,430.1,414.6,438.1 role=paragraph -->
representatives of identitarian thinking to which Deleuze’s Nietzsche is sup-

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,420.1,386.6,428.1 role=paragraph -->
posed to be opposed. Moreover, for Nietzsche, it is the finitude of force in

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,410.1,403.8,418.1 role=paragraph -->
conjunction with the infinity of time that necessitates the hypothesis of eter-

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,400.1,385.6,408.1 role=paragraph -->
nal recurrence (cf. 1968: §1066). Thus, in §1062 of Will to Power , Nietzsche

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,390.1,398.6,398.1 role=paragraph -->
warns against the temptation to conclude from the disqualification of tele-

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,380.1,406.6,388.1 role=paragraph -->
ology that becoming harbours a ‘miraculous power of infinite novelty in its

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,370.1,402.6,378.1 role=paragraph -->
form and states’. In what effectively amounts to a pre-emptive critique of

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,360.1,386.6,368.1 role=paragraph -->
Deleuze’s subsequent attempt to align the notion of will to power with

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,350.1,322.4,358.1 role=paragraph -->
Spinoza’s natura naturans and Bergson’s élan vital , Nietzsche writes: ‘[This] is

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,340.1,394.6,348.1 role=paragraph -->
still the old religious way of thinking and desiring, a kind of longing to

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,330.1,397.3,338.1 role=paragraph -->
believe that in some way the world is like the old beloved, infinite, bound-

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,320.1,442.6,328.1 role=paragraph -->
lessly creative God – that in some way, “the old God still lives” – that long-

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,310.1,302.6,318.1 role=paragraph -->
ing of Spinoza which was expressed in the words “ deus sive natura ” (he even

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,300.1,346.6,308.1 role=paragraph -->
felt “ natura sive deus ”)’ (1968: §1062). Keith Ansell-Pearson provides a criti-

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,290.1,422.6,298.1 role=paragraph -->
cally nuanced appraisal of Deleuze’s Nietzsche, specifically with regard to the

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,280.1,409.5,288.1 role=paragraph -->
topic of eternal recurrence, in his Viroid Life: Perspectives on Nietzsche and the

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,270.1,314.6,278.1 role=paragraph -->
Transhuman Condition , London: Routledge, 1997, esp. 42–7. However, else-

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,260.1,374.6,268.1 role=paragraph -->
where in the same book, Ansell-Pearson seems to endorse the Deleuzean

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,250.1,418.6,258.1 role=paragraph -->
interpretation of recurrence: ‘The repetition implicated in the eternal return

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,240.1,398.6,248.1 role=paragraph -->
is not the repeating of an original model since there is no original moment

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,230.1,398.6,238.1 role=paragraph -->
which can be subjected to a law of repetition. Eternal return already takes

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,220.1,406.6,228.1 role=paragraph -->
place within the element of difference and simulacra’ (Ansell-Pearson 1997:

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,210.1,422.6,218.1 role=paragraph -->
62). It is precisely this Deleuzean characterization of eternal recurrence – as

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,200.1,402.6,208.1 role=paragraph -->
the repetition of difference rather than identity – which we believe to be

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,190.1,362.6,198.1 role=paragraph -->
incompatible with Nietzsche’s own understanding of the doctrine.

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=82.6,180.1,398.6,188.1 role=citation -->
- 13. This is undoubtedly why it is a demon who first broaches the idea under the

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=82.6,160.1,410.6,178.1 role=paragraph -->
heading ‘The Heaviest Burden’ in The Gay Science , IV, §341. 14. ‘[M]y world has just become perfect, midnight is also midday, pain is also a

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,150.1,384.8,158.1 role=paragraph -->
joy, a curse is also a blessing, the night is also a sun’ Thus Spake Zarathustra ,

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=98.6,140.1,270.6,148.1 role=paragraph -->
IV, ‘The Intoxicated Song’ (1969: 331).

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=82.6,120.1,202.6,138.1 role=citation -->
- 15. Nietzsche 1968: §664–7. 16. Nietzsche 1968: §1011.

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=82.6,110.1,190.6,118.1 role=footnote -->
> [^note]: 17. Nietzsche 1968: §1067.

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=341.5,614.1,361.5,622.1 role=header -->
<!-- Notes -->

<!-- goidev:page=274 bbox=368.9,614.1,380.9,622.1 role=pagenumber -->
<!-- 259 -->

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=82.2,590.1,150.2,598.1 role=citation -->
- 18. Cf. Deleuze,

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=82.2,580.1,418.2,598.1 role=paragraph -->
Nietzsche and Philosophy , 171–5. 19. ‘[T]he world of “phenomena” is the adapted world which we feel to be real.

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,570.1,426.2,578.1 role=paragraph -->
The “reality” lies in the continual recurrence of identical, familiar, related

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,560.1,398.2,568.1 role=paragraph -->
things in their logicized character, in the belief that here we are able to

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,550.1,394.2,558.1 role=paragraph -->
reckon and calculate. […] The antithesis of this phenomenal world is not

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,540.1,394.2,548.1 role=paragraph -->
“the true world”, but the formless, unformulable world of the chaos of

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,530.1,418.2,538.1 role=paragraph -->
sensations – another kind of phenomenal world, a kind “unknowable” for us;

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,520.1,410.2,528.1 role=paragraph -->
[…] [Q]uestions, what things in themselves may be like, apart from our sense

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,510.1,402.2,518.1 role=paragraph -->
receptivity and the activity of our understanding, must be rebutted with the

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,500.1,418.2,508.1 role=paragraph -->
question: how could we know that things exist? “Thingness” was first created

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,490.1,402.2,498.1 role=paragraph -->
by us’ (Nietzsche 1968: §569). Such remarks provoke an obvious rejoinder:

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,480.1,398.2,488.1 role=paragraph -->
if, as Nietzsche so often insists, it makes no sense to talk about what the

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,470.1,414.2,478.1 role=paragraph -->
world is like independently of our relation to it, and ergo in abstraction from

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,460.1,394.2,468.1 role=paragraph -->
those things with which our senses and understanding reckon and calculate,

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,450.1,394.2,458.1 role=paragraph -->
then why even suppose there to be a ‘formless, unformulable’ and hence

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,440.1,402.2,448.1 role=paragraph -->
unknowable world beyond the world of identical, familiar, related, logicized

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,430.1,418.2,438.1 role=paragraph -->
things? Why suppose that a ‘chaos’ of sensations prior to their logicization

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,420.1,418.2,428.1 role=paragraph -->
as things exists? Moreover, the premise that this ‘chaos’ must be assumed to

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,410.1,386.2,418.1 role=paragraph -->
be the cause of our orderly, logicized sensations is insupportable given

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,400.1,426.2,408.1 role=paragraph -->
Nietzsche’s critiques of causality and his restriction of the notion of cause to

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,390.1,230.2,398.1 role=paragraph -->
the realm of logicized sensation.

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=82.2,380.1,410.2,388.1 role=citation -->
- 20. Nietzsche himself seems to have been perfectly aware of this: ‘What actually

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,370.1,385.6,378.1 role=paragraph -->
arouses indignation over suffering is not the suffering itself but the sense-

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,360.1,396.3,368.1 role=paragraph -->
lessness of suffering: but neither for the Christian, who saw in suffering a

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,350.1,390.2,358.1 role=paragraph -->
whole hidden machinery of salvation, nor for naïve man in ancient times,

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,340.1,414.2,348.1 role=paragraph -->
who saw all suffering in relation to spectators or to instigators of suffering,

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,330.1,412.6,338.1 role=paragraph -->
was there any such senseless suffering. […] “All evil is justified if a God

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,320.1,435.8,328.1 role=paragraph -->
takes pleasure in it”: so ran the primitive logic of feeling – and was this logic

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,310.1,386.2,318.1 role=paragraph -->
really restricted to primitive times? The gods viewed as the friends of cruel

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,300.1,390.2,308.1 role=paragraph -->
spectacles – how deeply this primeval concept still penetrates into our

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=98.2,290.1,286.2,298.1 role=paragraph -->
European civilization!’ (Nietzsche 1994: 48).

<!-- goidev:page=275 bbox=82.2,260.1,358.2,288.1 role=citation -->

<!-- goidev:page=276 bbox=82.6,500.1,402.6,518.1 role=citation -->
- 32. Cf. Hegel 1989: 390–408. 33. Realism of any sort never seems to have been a serious option for Nietzsche,

<!-- goidev:page=276 bbox=98.6,480.1,426.6,488.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=276 bbox=98.6,470.1,386.6,478.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=276 bbox=98.6,450.1,418.6,458.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=276 bbox=98.6,440.1,362.6,448.1 role=paragraph -->

<!-- goidev:page=276 bbox=98.6,430.1,398.6,438.1 role=paragraph -->
Schopenhauer, the will to power is at once the agent and patient of evalua-

<!-- goidev:page=276 bbox=98.6,420.1,178.6,428.1 role=paragraph -->
tion for Nietzsche.
